Environmental Impact Assessment
Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Executve Summary
Ei--XECUTIVESUMMARY
Infrastructure Leasing and Financial Services (IL&FS)- awarded
M/s. Kirloskar Consultants Limited, Pune the job to carry out the
detailed feasibility study of the widening and strengthening of
Vadodara-Halol Highway project for implementation of the project on
a commercial format. This also involved the environmental viability
study which included environmental baseline data generation,
preparation of an integrated Environmental and Social Impact
Assessment Report (ESAR) documenting major social issues,
consequent risks and the suitable action plans and also Environmental
Management Plan outlining the measures for improving the
environmental  quality  and  budgetary  cost  estimation  for
implementation.
- 1.0      PROJECT DESCRIPTION
1.1        Project Type
The total length of the project road is 33 km with the initial stretch from
km 8/300 to km 34/300 falling in Vadodara and Vaghodia tahsils of
Vadodara district while the remaining (km 3413= to km 40/300) falling in
Halol tahsil of Panchamahals district. This project being a highway and
bridge project has been classsified          r# as per The World
Bank categorization. The Roads and Building (R&B) Department,
::                    Govemrnment of Gujarat (GoG) and Infrastructure Leasing and Financial
Services Limited (IL&FS) have entered into a Memorapdum df
Agreement to develop and implement the widening / strengthening of
the Vadodara-Halol section of this state highway (SH87) on a
commercial format.
1.2        Need For The Project
The traffic studies identify that the-existing two lane carriageway
accommodates 18000 Passengers Car Unit (PCU) in a day, which
according to standards is close to capacity. The average journey speed
observed on the project road is 40 kmph which while considered with
limited scope for overtaking fix the level of service as 'poor. Delays are
experienced due to cattle crossing and all along the project road and
pedestrian crossing at the villages. The friction due to developments at
the villages contribute to the reduction in level of service and increase in
accidents at these locations. The single carriageway at the current level
of service where overtaking becomes risky, also contributes to the
reduction in level of safety on the project road.
" B Both these districts also wit*ss a high growth of Industries and Small
Scale Industrial (SSI) units on a consistent pace which could be largely
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
1



Environmental Impact Assessment
Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Executive Summary
attributed to the attractive policies floated by the governments. As a
result of growth induced industrial policies, large scale investments have
been forthcoming at a rapid rate.
Addition of a two lane carriageway with directional segregation is
therefore necessary to enhance the levels of service and safety for
projected future traffic.
1.3        Project Components
The project mainly consists of strengthening the existing carriageway
with a flexible overlay formation with two additional lanes seperated by a
median and provision of paved and earthen shoulders and provision of
service roads on either side. The bridges and culverts on existing
alignment are proposed to be improved or reconstructed depending on
the condition. Bridges and cross drainage works are to be constructed
-on the new formation. The borrow areas have been identified. The
quarries are located at Savalia and Halol which are established quarries
for metal. Sand is proposed to be brought from Goma river near HaloL
The various alignment options were exai  for minimizing the social
and environmental impacts at villages - Jarod, Asoj, Khandewadi and
Baska and suitable one was recommended
2.0        BASELINE ENVIRONMENTAL STATUS
The study area conmprised the area falling wi±in 5 km dJsaze on
either side of the proposed and existing alignment for the study of
various environmental attributes in general. For detailed studies an
area within 1 km distance on either side of the alignment was
considered. The Consultants had carried out Environmental Impact
Studies including baseline data ge.deration, secondary data
compilation to assess the impacts on environmental attributes during
construction and operation phase of the proposed project including the
impacts of quarrying operations.
The field studies were conducted for a period of one month from 24th
August to 25th September, 1996 to determine the existing conditions of
various environmental attributes viz., air quality, meterology, surface
and ground water quality, ecology, prevailing land use and industrial
activities along the project road and noise levels. Meterological data
was generated at Halol and Dhanjipura for one month.
Other studies such as land use pattem, socio-economic profile were
based on secondary data collected from various Government agencies
supplemented by limited field surveys carried out by the Consulatnts.
Comprehensive field observations were enlightened on evaluation of
alternative alignments at wherever there were problems of sensitive,
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
2



Environmental Impact Assessment
Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Executive Summary
social and technical issues such as existing topography, surface water
bodies and villages or sensitive areas.
2.1        Air Quality
Ambient Air Quality Monitoring (AAQM) stations were set up at seven
locations viz., Dhanjipur, Jarod, Asoj, Baska, Anandapura, Baska, Paladi
(for background concentration) and Halol for sampling SPM and NOx at
eight hourly interval. In order to facilitate calibration of CALINE3, an air
dispersion model. a straight stretch of road of length 1.2 km was
selected at two locations viz., Anandpura and Baska villages. Hourly
samples for NOx. CO and HC during peak traffic flow were taken at
different distances from centre of the road for three days. Hourly traffic
counts were also monitored at these locations concurrently with the
continuous monitoring. AAQM stations were also set up at the
predominant downwind locations to cross check as well as to facilitate
-U                    the calibration of the model.
The observations are based on a perusal of *h field campaign resuJts
are summarized below:
The sampling locations in residential areas (Dhanjipur, Asoj, Anandpura
and Baska) at about 30 m to 60 m away from the existing road indicates
average 24 hourly concentrations for SPMA -in    ae of 62.2 to 82.4
j.g / m3 and NOx in the range of 18.8 to 22.13 Mg! i3. The baseline
location at village Paladi which is about 1.5 km from the existing r4oad
indicates average concentrations for SPM and NOx as 54.2 jig/M3 and
11.3 .ggmh Tespectively. The aveage 24 hourly concentrativi,fr SPM
at highway junctions at Halol town and village Jarod are observed to be
105.5 Pg/M3 and 51.6 Zg/m3 respectively. The same for NOx is 22.1
gg/m3 and 18.5 tg/m3 respectively. One (peak) hourly average CO
levels at all the locations in the upwind and downwind direction were
observed to be below detectable limit of 1145 tg/m3. The maximum
concentration for hydrocarbons was observed to be in the range of 0.20
to 0.27 ppm in the upwind location, and 0.29 to 0.42 ppm in the
downwind locations.
To conclude the ambient air quality levels observed during the study
period are found to be well within the standards prescribed by Central
Pollution Control Board (CPCB) for residential and and commercial area.
2.2        Noise Level Survey
The noise monitoring has been conducted at Dhanjipur, a representative
of residential locality with a temple being a sensitive area in the vicinity;
near Halol Naka, a representative of the traffic flow at junction condition;
and near village Jarod in view of residential spread. The monitoring was

also carried out at the at the stretch of the road near village Anandpura
where traffic is smooth flowing and near a sharp curve at village Bhaska
to facilitate the calibration of noise model noise levels during peak traffic
hours (4 pm to 8 pm) at a distance of 15 and 30 meters from the edge of
the road.
a)   Daytime Noise Levels (Lday)
Residential zone: The day time noise level at the two locations is
observed to be exceeding the prescribed limit of 55 dB(A) at Jarod (62.9

highest level at 65.6 dB(A). High background noise levels in the

-Commercial Zone: The day time noise level at the location (Halol Naka)

prescribed limit of 65 dB(A). The noise levels recorded at this station
indicates a level of 65.8 dB(A).
b)   Night time Noise Levels (Lnighd

dB(A) to 67.4 dB(A). The high noise levfif we Gtributable to the traffic.

2.3        Water Quality
For the water quality survey, four sampling locations viz., Ka\handewadi,
Asoj, Baniyara and Hami villages for surface water and one viz., jarod
for ground water were selected.
All tested parameters are found well within the permissible limits as per
IS:2296 except for suspended solids and thereby indicating the good
quality of surface water at all sampling locations. In case of ground
water, Total Dissolved Solids (TDS) is observed to exceed the desirable
standard of 500 mg/l. All other parameters are found to be well within the
permissible limits of IS:10500.

2.4         Landuse Pattern
The data on landuse which is normally available has been collected from
various secondary sources like District Census Handbook, Revenue
records, State and Central Government Offices is for the village revenue
boundary. Agriculture is the predominant land use along the road for
over 20 km out of the 33 km stretch i.e. for more than 60% of the length.
Nearly 90% of the length of the road has agricultural and open land as
the adjoining land use.
2.5         Soil Characteristics
Most of the area adjoining the road is under cultivation and agriculture is
the main occupation of people in mte study area. Soil samples were
collected from agricultural fields at four locations viz.. Dhanjipur near
Oden chouk, Jarod, Asoj and Baska to establish the baseline
characteristics and to assess the agricultural and afforestation potential
of the soil.
Soil colour is observed to be varying between black to slightly black
brown. The texture is observed to be predamWi'sty clay followed by
clayey soils. This soils being derived from the weathering of Deccan
traps are deep retentive and fertile.
The soils in the area is observed to be slightly to moderately alkaline.
The Electrical Conductivity (EC) is obseived to bearagve The NitrDgen
levels fall under the category of "better to sufficient". Similarly, the
Potassium levels fall under the category of "very low to 'aedium". The
levels of Phosphorus fanl under the category of 'less to average
sufficient". The levels of organic matter in the soils fall under the
category of "sufficient to average sufficient".
2.6         Geology, Hydrology And Hydrogeology
Major portion of Vadodara district in western part is covered by thick pile
of alluvium. A major portion of Panchamahals district in the central and
northern parts is covered by meta sediments while the southern parts
are found with formations which have been referred to as the
Champaner series. In the eastern part of the distnct, Deccan Traps
occupy a large area and at places on their fringes are found in Infra-
Trappeans.
The Vishvamitri enters Vaghodia taluka and flows past Asoj, Jarod and
Kamrol villages. The surva river flows past Kotambi, Bhaniyara and
Morlipura villages in Vaghodia taluka and passes near Amliyara, Kotali,
and Sukhlipur in Vadodara taluka. There are a number of medium and -
minor irrigation crossings on the existing highway road. The main
crossings are at km 32/2 for the Narmada main canal of about 110 m top
Kirloskar consultants Limited
.: t~~~~~~~~~~~~~~



Environmental Impact Assessment
Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Executive Summary
width, and km 10/4 for the branch canal. The statistics available for
1986-87 show that wells are the main sources of irTigation.
The hydrological tests conducted on tubewells have indicated that the
alluvial aquifers are capable of transmitting large quantities of
groundwater in general. In the hilly and undulating terrain in the east and
comparatively flat terrain in the west of Panchmahals, adequate supply
of water may be obtained in shallow bore wells piercing the porous
horizons of lava flows and in flows traversed by closely spaced joints.
2.7        Terrestrial Ecology
The objectives of ecological study is to characterize the environmental
components like land, water, fora and fauna and to identify susceptible
and sensitive areas. The area adjoining the existing highway
(approximately 1-2 km on either side) has been considered for the
primary survey to study the terrestrial ecosystem.
l  ^               The area depicts a manmade terrestrial ecosystem  with natural
vegetation limited to small patches at certain places. The bio-diversity of
vegetation in the area is not very high He     intense biotic pressure.
There is no reserved forest, national park, wild life sanctuary or
biosphere reserve in the core area.
Within the thirty-one km stretch out of 1621 trees recorded 464 were
small, 735 were medium and 1422 were large trees. The study revealed
the existence of thirty eight species in 31 km patch of road. In all 1621
pIwTt3 mported, highest percentage was cd 7geem  TM7o); All

3.0        IMPACT ASSESSMENT
The environmental impacts based on environmental baseline conditions
and analysis of the field data are categorized as either primary (direct) or
secondary (indirect). The details on impact of the project activity on each
of the environmental attributes is summarized below.
3.1        Impact On Air Quality
* Construction Phase Impacts
Burning of diesel will be the principal cause of air pollution during the
construction phase. Under adverse meteorological conditions like
stable condition during winter after the sunset, it is possible that the
NO2 standards (80 Jtg/M3 for 24 hourly average) may be violated in
the construction yard if the construction woTr is carried out round the
clock. There will be also be a rise in SPM levels due to the construction
activities. The SPM standards, however, are nor expected to be
violated as the background levels are very low and the particulates
tend to sette during the low wind and stable conditions which give rise
to highJerels of gaseous poJJutants.
O Operational Phase
The air quality predictions carried out by using the air quality model
CALINE3 developed by California Department of Transportation for
years 2000 and 2015, respectively, have been considered to project
future air quality scenarios to provide an indication of long term
variations in air quality.
CO Levels:
It is observed that the predicted CO levels at 10 m from ROW for cross
winds in the year 2000 range form 160-55 g.g/m3. The range is about 3
times higher for the year 2015 which is approximately the ratio of traffic
volume in the years 2000 and 2015 respectively. On comparison with
the hourly standard for CO of 4000 pLg/m3, it is seen that no violations
of CO standard are expected due to the project. The CO levels in fact
will remain well below the standards. The project therefore has
insignificant negative impact on ambient air quality in terms of CO.

3.3        Impact On Water Quality
The construction and operation of the proposed highway project will not
have any major impact on the surface water and the ground water
quality in the area. Contamination to water bodies may result due to
spilling of construction materials, oil, grease, fuel and paint in the
equipment yards and asphalt plants. Impact on the ground water quality
is not anticipated from the project during the both construction and
operational phases as there is no case of dumping thereby leaching of
harmful ingredients down to water table.
3.4        Impact On Terrestrial And Aquatic Ecology
The initial constwction works at the project site involving land clearance,
cuffing, filling and leveling, will cause loss of potential agricultural
- v            productive land and loss of about 966 trees out of 1621 identified along
the road. Inevitably, there will be a shoi¶xteTn impact of availability of
nesting sites for the birds. Further, there is no sensitive ecological area
near the existing road, so the impact will not of significance. With the
landscaping after the project, the number of tees are expected to be
more than earlier numbers and will allow the short term impact to be
reversed.

of importance. Therefore, the impact of constructional activities on faur.a

5.1         Risk Assessment
The proposed alignment bypassing the villages viz., Jarod, Asoj,
Khandewadi and Baska thereby limiting the adverse impacts of air and
noise pollution within acceptable limits reduces the social risk as there
are minimal Resettlement and Rehabilitation (R&R) aspects to be
tackled. As the proposed project will be strengthening the existing Cross
Drainage (CD) works and proposing new ones adjacent to the existing
ones, hence the CD works is not an issue of significance in this project.
It is recommended that the following back-up facilities be provided as a
part of the project in the event of an accident due to continued and
increased transportation of hazardous substances;
i.   ambulance stationed at the toll booth
ii.   tow truck for removing damaged vehicles
iii.   communication facilities at each (and in between) ends
iv.   access to fire-local (district) fighting facilities.

6.0        OBJECTIVES OF SOCIAL ASSESSMENT STUDY
The main objective of the social assessment exercise is to assess the
social impact of the project, identify issues and consequent risks in the
resettlement and rehabilitation (R&R) of the project affected persons
(PAPs), outline R&R measures to mitigate any negative impact of the
project. In otherwords, the exercise has three main components: (i) a
General Social Impact Assessment, (ii) Public Information and
Consultation (PIC), the results of which will become the base for (ii) a
Resettlement Action Plan (RAP).
6.1         Benefits From The Project
All the road users, including the PAPs, will be benefited from the
proposed improvement on accout of comfort, safety, reduced travel
time, saving on vehicle operating costs due to enhanced speed, better
geometrics, and smooth surface. The project will upgrade linkages to
national highways and open up the area for increased economic
activities. A significant economic benefit of the project is the generation
of employment opportunities in the construction activities which will be
available to the people, including affected community.
7.0         SOCIO-ECONOMIC  ASPECTS  OF *TE  VILLAGES  IN  THE
PROJECT AREA
Villages within the 1 km stretch of the 9=iei study area on the either
side of the proposed alignment have been considered for socio-
-economic studtes. As per te village boundary maps depicted in 1991
Census handbooks, there are 12 villages in Vadodar (,iz..

therefore 14 villages in total falling within the study area.
The total population of the villages under consideration is observed to be
23,598. The males and females-onstitute to about 52.88% and 47.120,
of the study area population respectively. The density population reveals
that the study area has an overall density of 282 persons per km2. The
high density of population in the study area could be attributed to rapid
industrialisation and urbanization in the area.

9.0        SOCIO-ECONOMIC SURVEY
9.1        Families Affected
For initial alignment 594 structures in 31.5 kms road length were
identified. These consisted of 225 structures being used for residential
purposes, 245 commercial establishments, 59 community or public
infrastructure (including 19 temples) and 65 other structures. This
would have resulted in a major relocation problem in addition to huge
amounts of compensation payable to the owners of the structures. At
the same time this would have significantly disturbed the economic
living of the people. However after realigning the project road by
providing bypasses, subsequent to the socio-economic survey, only 10
structures mainly used for residential purposes are coming within the
ROW  (including bypasses) which need to be relocated outside the
project boundary. Two tenants and 1 squatter are residing in three
houses. Besides through these maaevs it was possible to avoid the
loss of many of the public utilities like bus stand, handpumps, water
tanks, panchayat biuldings etc. and cultural properties like temples
except three bus-stops and 7 small temples. The only commercial
structures requiring relocation are 11 movable shops. There are 10
residential structures and one defunct factory at Baska bypass coming
in the project area.
10.0       RESETTLEMENT ACTION PLAN
The basic objective of the Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) is to ensure
that the PAPs from the 'corridor of impact' of VHRP get adequate
comperisation and assistance so as to achieve or improve their pre-
project economical status in th.eshortest time and retain their potential
for further economic growth. The major components of the RAP are
described in following sections.
10.1       Loss of Land
Considering the project design and its deviations from the ROW, the
VHRP covering 31.5 km of road improvement will require acquisition
of an estimated 107.93 ha of land in Vadodara and Panchmahal
districts.
In the absence of information about the profile of land tenants, the
entitlement for tenants is based on the estimated land under tenancy
from the plot numbers identified. The total land under tenancy is
estimated as 25.45 ha (16.72 ha. - irrigated and 7.73 ha.- un-irrigated).
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
14



Environmental Impact Assessment
Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Executive Summary
Since only severely affected tenants are to be provided land equal to
MEL, it is assumed that such requirements will be limited to 10 tenants
and the remaining will be assisted through loans.
Only one case of industrial land loss of a defunct factory at Baska
village exists. The compensation will be paid after its engineering
valuation.
In case of the govemment land, since the GoG is a member
(shareholder) of SPV, and since the project (road) will be transferred
back to the government, no compensation is proposed to be paid for
such government lands. As the gram panchavat / government land
holding is yet to be identified through Joint Measuring Survey (JMS), it
is proposed to pay compensation to the Gram Panchayat as alienation
charges from the contingency provision.
10.2       Loss of Structures
As per IL&FS policies, all house owners are compensated by a house
-2            ~ of area equal to the area of the lost structure. In case the area of the
house is less than the minimum government norm for single family
dwelling, a house of minimum area is provided findira Awas Vikas
Yojana). For squatters, irrespective of the size fcr the present dwelling,
an ownership house of minimum area as per government norm is
provided. The beneficiary under this case however cannot sell the
house till completion of the concession period of the project.
In the case of tenants, it is expected that they will re-establish
themselves elsewhere and the IL&FS policy entitles them only for a
shff&QaanCe. However inte present cases. the tenar snrD
ecorn-3vnally weaker sections anid 9amviig in damaged;muffs due to
financial constraints. It is difficult to envisage that they will be able to
shift to altemate accommodation and re-establish themselves. As a
special case, therefore, these tenants will also be provided with a
dwelling unit of minimum area. The house owner will be compensated
for the land and structure based on valuation.
A shifting allowance of Rs. 1000 will be provided to each of the 10
PAPs.
10.3       Institutional Arrangements
There is a need to focus on two main issues:
* Issues related to LA and alternate land allocation for severely
affected PAPs; and
v  Issues related to Economic Rehabilitation (ER) of PAFs.
Land acquisition (LA) is primarily the government responsibility and a
mutually acceptable compensation at market rate is to be arrived at
through negotiations. The SPV is committed to pay the difference
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
15 -



Vadodara - Ha/al Road                                   Executive Summary
between the compensation amount as per govemment rates and the
negotiated settlement price of the land. The procedure therefore will
involve interaction and consensus building with the PAFs on an
agreeable fair rate of compensation. LA for the project is the
responsibility of the Revenue Department which is done through a
Special Deputy Collector or District Land Acquisition Officer (LAO) at
the district level. Thus, a district-wise Compensatory Land
Procurement Committee for each of the two districts is proposed to
facilitate identification, purchase and transfer/allocation of land to the
severely affected PAPs.
Economic Rehabilitation (ER): This is one of the critical areas of
project implementation. It is proposed to set up two committees: (i)
Project Level R&R Monitoring Committee to ensure that the pronosed
ER measures are being implemented as planned, make policy
decisions to facilitate implementation of the same and give necessary
administrative decisions/directions as required; and (ii) District Level
R&R Implementing Committee to implement the identified ER program.
In this context, it is necessary that a NGO experienced in tA
procedures and in implementing income generation schemes work
closely with SPV and governmern. Thus the implementation
responsibility through an institutional arrangement will need to be
developed around the SPV, GoG Representative and NGO
involvement.
10.4       Implementation Schedule
The first activity of the project, as far as LA is concemn4 is the issue of
a notification u/s 4(1) of the LA Act, 1894 as amended in 1984.
Compensation needs to be paid to the PAPs before the start of actual
project construction. In case of any delay in LA proceedings, R&B will
take lands from their owners on 'consent basis'. The implementation
schedule is reflected in the annual phasing of budget funds wherein it
is assumed that 75% of land acquisition and related ER will be done in
the first year itself, while the bafince will be done in the second year.
10.5       Monitoring and Evaluation
Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) will be done simultaneously with the
implementation of the LA & RAP. Besides conventional government
monitoring, an extemal agency will be engaged to meaningfully and
realistically evaluate R&R programmes on periodic basis. Further
arrangements for M&E will be at project level (by SPV) and district
level (by  District Level ER  Implementation  Committees  and
Compensatory Land Acquisition Committee).

1.0        INTRODUCTION
1.1        BACKGROUND
The Vadodara - Halol Road which links Vadodara city to the industrial
town of Halol had been identified as one of the most important State
Highways in the State of which requires immediate upgradation by the
strategic options study of the state road network instituted by the
Government of Gujarat in 1995. This road also forms part of the route
to the states of Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan and Delhi from southem
parts of Gujarat and Maharashtra. The Roads and Building (R&B)
IF =Department, Government of Gujarat (GoG) and Infrastructure Leasing
and Financial Services Limited (IL&FS) have entered into a
Memorandum  of Agreement to  develop  and  implement the
widening/strengthening of the Vadodara-Halol section of this state
highway (SH87) on a commercial format. The project road extends
from the intersection of Vadodara bypass to the intersection of the
Halol bypass. The IL&FS had made a preliminary feasibility study of
the proposed project in early 1996 with technical assistance from
Kirloskar Consultants Limited. The preliminary feasibility study covered
limited traffic surveys, estimation of tiravff;e h rates, collection of
road inventory data, rough cost estimation, assessment of willingness
.to pay toll, determination of toll, financi mid economic analysis,
preliminary environment and social assessme= and identification of
risk parameters. The preliminary feasibility study has concluded that to
service the projected traffic, four laning of &,-e road is needed and the
project is economically viable. The preliminary financial analysis
indicated that the project can be taken up on a commercial format.
%fl1sk=C==ftants Limited is retained by the IL&TS to cXany out the
4etaw4ed 4easibility study of this ,,.jtfor implemerntation of tiL project
on a commercial format. The setting of the Project Road and the study
area are shown in Figure 1.1 and Figure 1.2 respectively. The project
proposal involves enhancing the carriageway capacity to provide a high
level of service to the traffic. The users will be charged a toll to recover
the cost of implementation of the project.
Besides examining the technical, economical and financial feasibility of
the proposed project, the study aims at carrying out environmental and
social impact assessment of the proposed project to ascertain if any
alignment modification is required, to identify environmental and social
risks involved and the mitigation measures thereof.
Kirloskar Consultants Limited

1.2        OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF THE DETAILED FEASIBILITY
STUDY
The main objectives of the study (which includes traffic studies with
preliminary engineering for technical options, environmental impact
assessment) as defined in the Terms of Reference (TOR) are:
to carry out a feasibility study for the proposed road project to establish
its technical, environmental, economic and financial viability; and
.  to undertake risk identification and -assessment, including risk analysis.
The technical studies involve necessary field investigation and surveys
for. preparing preliminary engineering design of the project and
estimating the project cost to a level of accuracy of +/- 10%. The
environmental impact assessment study is expected to prepare a well
researched and documented environment impact assessment report
(EIA) with due consideration to public opinion and sensitivities. The
EIA  Study follows the guidelines stipulated by the Ministry of
Environment and  Forests  (MoEF), Government of India for
environmental impact assessment of highway projects.
1.3        SPECIFIC SCOPE OF ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT (EA)
STUDY
A 5 km radius area along the proposed and existing alignment is
considered for the study of various environmental attributes in general.
For detailed studies an area within 1 km distance on -either side o7 the
alignment is considered.
The broad scope of the study is:
to conduct a literature review and to collect additional data
relevant to the study area;
*    to undertake environmental monitoring so as to establish the
baseline environmental status of the study area;
*    to assess the impacts on environmental attributes due to the
construction and operation of the proposed project road including
the impacts of quarrying operations on the existing environmental
status of the area;
* .    to prepare an Environmental Management Plan (EMP) outlining
the measures for improving the environmental quality and
budgetary cost estimation for implementation;

1.4        THE PROJECT AREA
The proposed project road passes through two districts viz., Vadodara
and Panchmahals of Gujarat state. Vadodara district is situated in
eastem Gujarat between rivers Narmada and Mahi. Vadodara is the
12m" largest district in the state. Measuring 7,788 sq.km. and
accounting for 3.97% of the state's total geographical area, it lies
betwen 210 00' and 230 00' North and 740 00' and 740 10' East
longitudes. It is bounded on the north by Panchmahals district, on the
north-west by Kheda district, on the south and south-west by Bharuch
district, on the east by Madhya Pradash Sate and the south-east by
the Dhulia district of Maharashtra state. The district is formed by twelve
talukas including Vadodara. Situated on t   g .-k of Vishvamitr,
Vadodara city is the third largest city in the state with an area of 670
sq.km constituting about 8.6% of the district area. The district takes its
name from its only city and its largest urban center, Vadodara city.
The district Panchmahals is called so, because it originally consisted of
5 mahals, viz., Godhra, Kalol, Halol, Dohad and Jhalod. It
approeimately lies between 730 15' and 740 30' East loaguiMde and 200
30' and 230 30' North latitude- Jt ba an area of 8866 sq.km and 11
tahsils including Halol and Godhra. It is bounded on the north by
Sabarkantha district of Gujarat state and the Banswara district of
Rajasthan, to the west by Vadodara and Kaira districts, to the south by
the Vadodara district and the Madhya Pradesh.
On the basis of physiographic setup, Vadodara district has been
divided in two zones viz., the eastiem hilly zone comprising Chhota
Udeour, Jabugam and Navadi talukas and the rest level plain and
undulating area. Except the westem part comprising Kalol and parts of
Halol, Godhara which constitute a plain country, Panchmahals district
is a hilly tract. Near Halol the plain is dissected by rivers and breaks
into the spurs and ridges, that latitudes centers in the massive hill of
Pavagadh (829.36 m above MSL).
The climate in the districts is characterized by a hot summer, and
general dryness except during the monsoon season. The average
annual rainfall in the districts vary between 953.8 mm to 988.0 mm.
Major portion of the districts is covered by thick pile of alluvium.

1.5        STUDY APPROACH
Reconnaissance survey was conducted by the consultants to identify
the major environmental issues. Accordingly field surveys were
undertaken to determine the ambient air quality, meteorological
conditions (concurrent to the air quality studies), water quality,
ecological status, noise levels, soil characteristics, prevailing land use
and collection of data on existing industries along the project road. The
field studies were supported by data collection from secondary sources
for meteorology, land use, hydrogeology and socio-economic aspects.
The sampling locations were identified on the basis of:
* predominant wind directions in the study area as recorded by Indian
-Meteorological Department (IMD) at Vadodara;
* existing topography and location of surface water bodies like ponds,
canals and rivers;
-  location of villages/towns/sensitive areas;
- areas which represent baseline conditions and pollution pockets in
the area;
* accessibility of the sites and availabi4 gf power for the monitoring
equipment; and
. collation and analysis of secondary data on baseline conditions for
various environmental attributes.
The field studies wem conducted for a peTii tf orn mDnth to
determine the existing conditions of various environmeniaibutes as
outlined in Table-1.1. The monitoring stations were commissioned on
24h August, 1996 and were operated till 25t" September, 1996.

IL&FS is committed to provide value added services in its mandated
areas of operation. The institution believes that improved access to
efficient public infrastructure necessitates synergy between prudent.
use  of natural resources  and  meeting  people's concerns. Thus,
attaining  environmental  and  social .viability  through  consultative

principles:
* enhance quality of life and environment in -and around the project
area by conserving natural resources and addressing the legitimate
concerns of relevant stakeholders, especially project affected

1.6.2.3    Movement Of Hazardous Chemicals
Movement of hazardous chemicals by road is governed by Central
Motor Vehicle Rules, 1989 (rules 129 through 137). Besides
regulations and precautions to be taken while transporting such goods,
the rules stipulate availability of a Transport Emergency Card
(TREMCARD) with the driver of the carrier which shall provide
information on hazardous nature of the chemical carried and also
precautions required to handle emergencies such as spillage and fire.
The carriers are also expected to prominently exhibit warning signs to
inform the public about the hazardous character of their cargo.
1.7        ORGANISATION OF REPORT
The Environment Impact Assessment Report comprises six chapters. A
brief description of the subject marter of various chapters is presented
below.
Chapter 1  -    Introduction
This chapter provides background information of the project, brief
description and objectives of the project, description of the project area,
scope and methodology of the study. It also provides information on key
environmental legislation and the standards relevant to attributes of
concern in the project.
Chapter 2  -    Project Details
This chapter bnefly presents the bacdiu-d fntmnation on the project
altematives evolved through engineering design considerations. The
altematives were screened for environmental and social impacts. Some
of those which were likely to result  in adverse impacls of larger
magnitude, where options were also available, were omitted. The
suggested altemative - She Project" - based on these considerations is
discussed in this chapter giving its major components. The design
standards and construction methods are also included. Traffic
projections are also summarised in this chapter.
Chapter 3  -    Baseline Environmental Status
This chapter presents the methodology and findings of field studies
undertaken with respect to ambient air, water, soils, noise levels and
ecology to define the existing environmental status in the area. It also
presents the meteorological conditions which govern the air quality
impacts, a major concem during the construction and operation of the
road. The location of industries along the existing road and the existing
land use are depicted based on a reconnaissance survey. Other relevant
secondary data are included on geology, hydrology, hydrogeology and
ecology from published information. The major social issues are also
discussed with specific reference to socio-economic aspects and land
acquisition.

Chapter 4  -    Impact Assessment
This chapter details the inferences drawn from the environmental impact
assessment of "The project" during construction and operation phase. It
describes the overall impacts of the proposed project and underscores
the areas of concem which need mitigation measures. A summary of
the construction and operation phase impacts are presented employing
the matrix methodology.
Chapter 5  -    Project and No Project Scenarios
This chapter provides a comparison, of the project and no-build
alternatives based on their overall environmental impacts.
Chapter 6  -    Environmental Management Plan (EMP)
This chapter provides recommendations for Environmental Management
Plan (EMP) including landscaping for minimising the negative
environmental impacts of the project. Environmental monitoring
requirements for effective implementation of mitigatory measures during
construction as well as operation of the project have also been
delineated along with required institutional arrangements for their
implementation. Budgetary cost estimates for mnitigation measures are
also brought out. Risk assessment is included from the environmental
perspective. Action Sheets are given to facilitate implementation of the
EMP.

discussed with specific reference to land acquisition, R&R of PAPs,
strategy for implementation, institutional suppotl. mimriftnO aid
evaluation.
Annexure II -    Public Information and Consultations
The PIC meetings were conducted in six villages to ascertain the
concerns of the people in the context of the project. The brief findings
are presented as a prelude to the RAP.
Appendices
Appendices are included at the end to present the detailed data
collected during the course of the study.
Questionnaire
MoEF Questionnaire has been prepared as per the guidelines
prescribed for environmental appraisal of highway projects.

2.0         PROJECT DETAILS
2.1         NEED FOR THE PROJECT
The traffic studies identify that the existing two lane carriageway
accommodates 18000 PCU in a day, which according to standards is
close to capacity. The average journey speed observed on the existing
road is 40 kmph, which when considered with limited scoc:e for
overtaking, fix the level of service as 'poor'. Delays are experienced due
to cattle crossing all along the project road and pedestrian cross ng at
the villages. The friction due to developments at the villages contrinute to
the reduction in level of service and increase in accidents at *hese
locations.- The single carriageway at the current level of service .vhere
overtaking becomes risky, also contributes to the reduction in level of
safety on the project road. Addition of a two lane carriagewa, with
directional segregation is therefore necessary to enhance the le.-els of
service and safety for projected future traffic.
-   2.1.1      Industrial Activity
Due to growth inducing industrial policies enforced by successive state
govemments, Vadodara district shows subs;i  rowth in working
factories between 1970 and 1980, a stagnation between 1980 anc 19990
and significant growth since 1990. The district of Panchamahals snow a
significant growth between 1980 and 1990 wlar-h is naintained n 90's
also.
Both these districts also witness a high growth of Small Scale Inaustrial
(SSI) units at a consistent pace which could be largely atnrhuted to the
attractive pblicies floated by tte gvnm ents. But, they do not show
prominence of any specific industry group. There are large number of
chemical, metal and engineering units in these districts.
2.1.2       Future Industrial Growth
As a result of growth induced industrial-policies, large scale inves-rnents
have been forthcoming at a rapid rate. As of now, about 2200 projects
with an estimated investment of Rs. 899.73 billion generating 420.000
jobs are under implementation in Gujarat. Among the project inf iJence
districts, Vadodara tops with a share of 170% of projects. Panchar-rahal's
share is about 3.5%.

The unrestricted growth traffic forecast indicates that the tota! traffic
volume will reach a level of 178,494 PCU by the year 2030. The details
are indicated in Table-2. 1 and also depicted in Figure-2. 1.

The objective of the project is to enhance the level of service of the
Project Road from  the current poor' to good' and sustain it at
acceptable levels in the next 20 to 30 years. To prepare a project
design which would meet the project objective, examination of the
technical / engineering alternatives for the following is considered
essential:
* Level of service criteria:
* Alignment options based on
- geometrics
- environmental and social aspects,
- Intersections;
*  Highway crossing;
* Pavement; and
& Cross drainage works.
Three options (see section 2.2.2 below) are also discussed therein to
increase the Level of Service and Safety by addition of a two lane
carriageway (to the existing two lane highway) with directional
segregation so as to provide a 4-lane dividtd =T-riageway with a design
speed of 100 kmph and also control access to the road and implement
the tolling concept. Alternatives arising o¶ vf engineering design
considerations are discussed in the Main Repot.
2.2.2       Project Options
The traffic studies identify that the existing two lane carriageway
accommodates 18000 PCU in a day, which according to standards is
closew to  Xc     and much above the design servioe   ke. The
average journey speed observad on Xhe Project Road is 4D kmph,
which, while considered with limited scope for overtaking, fix the level
of service as 'Poor'. The friction due to developments at the villages
contribute to the reduction in level of service and increase in accidents
at these locations. The single carriageway at the current level of
service, where overtaking becomes risky, also contributes to the
reduction in level of safety on the ProLect Road.
The initial project concept involved widening and strengthening of the
existing road to a 4 lane divided carriageway with a design speed of 100
kmph and recovering the cost through charging toll. Later the project
concept has been broadend to include provision of service roads to
provide added benefits to the local users.
Addition of a two lane carriageway with directional segregation will
enhance the levels of service and safety to very good standards for the
existing traffic. It is estimated that this addition will sustain a fair level
of service for about 20 years based on the traffic forecast. From the
points of view of the effect of direct access on the level of service,
segregation of slow moving vehicles and the requirements of the local
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
2-5



Environmenta7 impact Assessment                                   Chapter 2
Vadodara - Halol Road                                        Project Details
population, there are primarily two options of carriageway configuration
(variants to these primary options are considered to address the toll
concepts) for accomplishing this expansion.
Options of carriageway configuration:
Option 1, envisages strengthening and widening the Project Road to a
4-lane divided carriageway with provision of service road only at
locations with continuous development, as in villages and in a cluster of
factories. This will entail partial access control. The right-of-way
requirement and the organisation. of various ROW  elements of the
Option 1 are shown in Figure-2.3.
Option 2, envisages provision of a service road of single lane
carriageway on either side for the full lenqth of the Project Road. This
will. provide full access control and segregation of slow moving
vehicies. The full access control and traffic segregation will improve the
level of service and safety. The right-of-way (ROW) requirement and
the organisation of various ROW elements of the Option 2 are shown
in Figure-2.4.
There is a road side development at few locations along the Project
Road where restriction to ROW  requiO ts may be warranted to
minimize the displacement of people/struc±w-s. The ROW requirement
at such locations may be limited to 36 m as shown in Figure-2.5.
Option 3 will have the same specifications as in Option 2 for the main
carriageway, but the provision of service rad will not be for the full
length but only at places required to meet the access control
requirements and the movement of local trips of non-commercial
vehicles and trips within the Project Road limits. As in the case of
Option 2, it is observed that for Option 3, the carriageway of 60 m is
required and needs to be reduced to 36 m at few locations (Figure-
2.5).

Examination of the alignment altematives in the context of social and
environmental impacts is of significance at this detailed feasibility stage
since the realignment arising out of R&R and environmental issues was
not addressed at the prefeasibility stage.
The section below summarizes the initial assessment from
environmental and social angle for the alignment along the existing
carriageway as originally suggested by GoG. It is observed that out of a
total stretch of 33 kms., except at four road sections totaling
approximately 5 kms., highway for 28 kms can be designed to run
along the existing carriageway. However at villages viz., Jarod, Asoj,
Khandewadi and Baska, major difficulties arising due to the following
reasons were anticipated:
1. friction due to pedestrian interferences;
2. loss of residential, commercial and religious structures leading to
Resettlement and Rehabilitation (R&R) issues; and
3. environmental and social impaiaes.
The environmental and social impacts aig out of the improvement at
curves on the horizontal alignment along the existing carriageway
running through Jarod (Figure-2.6, Asoj (Figure-2.7), Khandewadi
(Figure-2.8) and Baska (Figure-2.9) with restricted ROW of 36 m and
fulfilling the requirement of the design speed of 100 kmph have been
summarized in Table-2.3.

2.3         RECOMMENDED ALIGNMENT FOR THE PROJECT
The horizontal alignment for the Project has been recommended based
on the detailed studies on alignment in the preceding sections and other
engineering considerations. The subsequent Environmental Assessment
is done with respect to this as the 'Project' and is considered as "Build
Option". A comparison of the "Build" and No Build" options is presented
in Chapter 5 after assessing the -environmental impacts of the Project
Road. Option 3 has been found suitable and thus selected for the
carriageway.
2.3.1       Project Components
The project mainly consists of strengthening the existing carriageway
with a flexible overlay formation with two additional lanes separated by a
median and provision of paved and earthen shoulders. A typical cross-
section is for the base case shtn_ -in jFigar-2.4. In the base case in
addition to the above service roads of single lane carriageway will be
provided on either side. The geome. -:fovements require total
realignment at few locations.
2.3 2       Earthwork
Since there is no cut areas, the soil required for the Project needs to be
obtained locally from borrow areas. The probable boro am-as
identified in the close vicinity along the existing highway through
reconnaissance survey are shown in Figure-2.10 and given in Table-2.6
(the suitability or otherwise of the soil of these areas has been
confirmed through laboratory tests).
The overall material requirement for earthfill material and stone
aggregates for Option 3 have been estimated to be about 0.7 million
tonnes and 1.2 million tonnes respectively. The stone aggregate will be
taken from the established Sawaliya quarry which is located at about 30
km from Halol. In addition to the above, sand (0.4 million tonnes) will be
taken from Goma river which is about 15 km from Halol towards
Pavagadh.

All the borrow areas identified are in land under the possession of the
government (either state or local-self government). Five such areas fall
on land where there is no cultivation. For the areas at the boundaries of
tanks in each village, the village chiefs want the tanks to be expanded
for enhancement of storage and if it could be established at no cost to
the Panchayat they would be prepared to allow removal of soil from the
area adjacent to the tanks. It is desirable to avoid borrow pits in the
ROW as the pits, if not restored to the original condition, can affect the
subgrade and the pavement, should water accumulates in the open
borrow pits.
2.3.3       Design Provisions for Access and Crossing
Access to lndividual Properties:
In the case of Option 3, the access between service roads must be a
grade separated facility, one in every five kilometres approximately.
Alternatively, the crossing can be provided by manned crossings where
the expected crossing volume is very low. Accordingly, grade
separated crossings are proposed at km 11/250 and two each at the
three villages of Jarod, Asoj and Baska. At-grade manned crossings
are proposed at km 18/0 and km 33/300. Al a-gabe crossings, traffic
from service road will not be allowed to enter the main carriageway. A
secondary toll plaza is proposed in the Option 3 at km 22/500 where
..avli Road and Waghodia Road joins the Project Road.
Access Between Carriageways:
In Option 3, the access between the carriageways through median
openings need to be very limited because of the service road provision.
2.3.4       Bridges And Culverts Design Options
There are 17 RCC pipe drains, 12 RCC slab drains, 3 minor bridges
and 3 major bridges along the existing roaa. The project involves work
of the following types as far as the cross drainage works and bridges
are concemed:
. Repair of existing CD works and bridges. including widening of
. carriage-way;
• Reconstruction of existing bridges CD works where warranted;
* Construction of new bridges and CD works for the additional two
lanes;
* Construction of new bridges CD works for the service roads;
* Construction of additional CD works if warranted; and
. There are 20 additional balancing culverts proposed to effectively
address the drainage issues.
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
2-21



-znvironmentai 7mpact Assessment                                     Chapter 2
Vadodara - Halol Road                                          Project Details
2.3.5       Designated Pedestrian Crossings
There are 14 villages along the Project Road and the people in these
villages cross the road for various purposes such as for water, cattle
feeding, agricultural needs, using the ponds etc. There are four cart
tracks crossing the bypass alignment at Jarod village. The Project
Road is proposed to be improved to have a travel speed of 100 kmph
which increase the danger to pedestrians crossing the Project Road.
Two alternatives have been considered and they are (1) pedestrian
underpass I overbridge and (2) designated pedestrian crossing. The
provision of pedestrian underpass/overbridge is costly and its utilisation
is debatable and hence the Consultants recommend provision of
designated pedestrian crossing or underpasses. The traffic shall be
warned of designated pedestrian crossings with traffic signs and
-focused lighting arrangement. The focused lighting arrangement wMil
reduce the possibility of accidents in the night. It is proposed to
determine the need and location of such crossings through Public
- Information and Consultations and supplemented by a reasonable
criteria of providing such crossings in the absence of specific demand.
2.3.6       Otlher Requirements
The approximate quantum of water required for the project is 330
million liters. The precise source of watertias not been identified in the
feasibility study, but it is assumed that the lead for water is within 2
kms. Considering the use of ponds ~a Asoj and Khandewadi.
exclusions are suggested in the environment management plan.
The total labour requirement is about 60,000 man days, whici is about
250-300 persons per day during the peak period. There .eeds to be
minimum of two labour camps with 100-1 50 people. However, details like
the number of labour, location of the labour camps, location and number
of asphalting plants etc. can only be decided on finalization of the
contract (when schedule of execution of the work based on capacity of
the contractor(s) to take up different sections at the same time, type of
equipment used etc. are known).-
2.3.7       Schedule for Implementation
The construction period for the project is expected to be two years..
Detailed schedule of major construction activities is given in the Table-
2.7. This was proposed to be started in Nov. 97 as per the original
schedule. However, the revised schedule will be on similar lines after
completion of the pre-project formalities. Thus the table is only indicative
of the nature of the activities and their likely duration.

3.0         BASELINE ENVIRONMENTAL STATUS
3.1        INTRODUCTION
This chapter incorporates the description of the existing environmental
setting in the study area for the project which extends upto a distance of
1 km on either side of the alignment for conducting detailed studies and
5 km on either side of the alignment for collecting data from secondary
sources. The total length of the project road is 33 km with the initial
stretch from km 8/300 to km 34/300 falling in Vadodara and Vaghodia
tahsils of Vadodara district while the remaining (km 34/300 to km
40/300) falling in Halol tahsil of Panchamahals district.
Reconnaissance and survey of the study area was carmed out by the
consuTtants team in the month of August, 1996 for the selection and
finalization of the sampling locations. Field monitorina for various
environmental attributes started from 24h August, 1996. Continuous
monitoring of meteorological parameters has been carned out for a
period of one month at two stations to establish the meteorological
conditions existing at the time of the sut-ve. VvriitoTing for ambient air
quality, noise levels and continuous monitoring of NOx. CO and HC
levels during peak traffic periods was carried wit -at various locations at
selected sampling frequencies. Traffic count sturIies also formed a part
of the field surveys which were helpful in conrelating the monitoring
results. Samples for water and soil w-a- ro4lw-t  Surveys were
-             conducted for assessing the ecological status and location of industries
along the alignment. Data from secondary sources has been compiled
for hydrogeology, landuse socio-economic and cultural aspects aspects
to supplement the field data in presenting.the baseline envifonmental
status of the area.
3.2         METEOROLOGY
The micro-meteorological parameters regulate the transport and
diffusion of pollutants released into the atmosphere. The principal
variables which affect the micro-meteorology are wind speed. wind
direction, atmospheric stability, inversion conditions and topography of
the area.
The Indian Meteorological Department (IMD) broadly divides the year
into four seasons as under:
Winter season -         December to February
i:::J*  Pre-monsoon season -    March to June
Monsoon season -        June to September
.     Post Monsoon Season -   October to November
Meteorological data collected from secondary sources (nearest IMD
station) provides long term data which is good for averaging purposes,
while the data generated at site is useful to characterise the
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
3-1



Environmental Impact Assessment                                           Chapter 3
Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Baseline Environmental Status
meteorological conditions prevailing at the time of the survey. These are
given below.
3.2.1        Data Collection from Secondary Sources
The meteorological data for the perod 1986-95 has been procured for
IMD, Vadodara, from IMD, Pune which is the central repository -of all
meteorological data generated in India. The IMD station at Vadodara is
located at about 2 km from the Golden chowk in the south-west
*                     direction. The  station  maintains wind  speed  and  wind  direction
information for eight directions, maximum and minimum temperature,
monthly mean humidity levels, atmospheric pressure, evaporation rate
and rainfall data. The data is recorded at 0830 and 1730 hrs.

3)    Cloud Cover
Clear bright weather prevails with clear to lightly clouded (1-2 octas)
skies for more than 24 days in a month during post monsoon season
(October - November) and winter season (December - February), During
early premonsoon season (April - May) a similar situation prevails. It is
only the monsoon period (June - September) that moderate (3-5 Octas)
to heavy (6-8 Octas) clouded skies are commonly observed for more
than 20 days in a month due to the southwest monsoon. Monthly
variations of cloud cover is presented in Appendix 3. 1
4)    Upper Air Data
The upper air climatological data assumes significance as the
assessment of air pollutants dispersion is influenced by these conditions.
The nearest station to Vadodara recording upper winds, is Ahmedabad
situated at a distance of 80 km in the North of Vadodara and the same
data has been presented for the analysis as the upper air data will not
change significantly at this distance for the present case.
Appendix 3.1 presents details of data available on the following:
* ground based inversions
* elevated inversions
* mixing heights
Data lor The oc=.rrence of ground based inverskns is available for 00
GMT i.e., 5.30 am and 12 GMT i.e., 5.30 pm for Ahmedabad. Ground
based inversions tend to prevail more at 5.30 am in post rnons on and
winter seasons (November - March) and least in the monsoon period. In
the evening, however, the ground based inversions are almost absent.
Elevated inversions at Ahmedabad whose base starts at heights below
100 m tend to occur more at 5.30 am in the months of October and
February - April. The same are almost absent in the evening at 5.30 pm.
Secondary data on mixing heights for Ahmedabad compiled by Mr. B.
Satyanarayana, Department of Meteorology and Oceanography, Andhra
University are available for morning at 8.30 am. The mean monthly
mixing height is the least in the month of December (90 m) while it is
highest in the month of August-September (210 m).

3.2.5       Observations Based on Field Campaign
The Indian Meteorological Department (IMD) records the data at two
times a day viz., 0830 hrs and 1730 hrs. The site specific data have
been recorded at hourly interval. On comparison of site specific data
generated for one month vis-a-vis the IMD data, slight variations were
found. The following observations are brought out:
*    The temperature recorded on site compared vis-a-vis the 10
years [MD data, slight variations in temperature levels were
*                          found. The temperatures recorded at site were in the range of
30.4°C to 32.5CC. whereas the value recorded at (MD is 31.5-C.
Evaporation rates in the range of 5.4 to 6.1 mm/day were
recorded at site during the study period, whereas the values
recorded at IMD is 4.6 mm/day;
Slight variations ware observed in case of wind speeds and
direction. The variations may be due to location, frequency and
time of recording and height of the station, which is different
nif  iiviv dan; ard
*    The data generated at continuous monitoring station when
compared with the data recorded al 4AD are found to be fairly
consistent. Based on the above analysis, one can conclude that
data generated on site is    dtaypatible with the regional
meteorology and therefore, data collected from IMD station can
be used for predicting air pollution level during the initial period.
3.3         AIR OIJALf¶Y
The ambient air quality with respect to the study zone of 5 km
surrounding the existing and proposed road forms the baseline
information The study area represents mostly rural environment with
about 40 industrial establishments along the road. The various sources
of air pollution in the region are vehicular traffic, dust arising from
unpaved village roads, domesfic fuel buming and industrial emissions.
The prime objective of baseline air quality survey is to assess the
existing air quality of the area. This will also be useful is assessing the
conformity to standards of the ambient air quality during the operation of
the road.
This section describes the selection of sampling locations, including the
me-hodology of sampling and analytical techniques with frequency of
sa mpling. The results of the survey are also presented.

3.3.1       Methodology
3.3.1.1    Selection of Sampling Locations
The baseline status of the ambient quality has been assessed through
an ambient air quality monitoring network. The design of monitoring
network in the air quality surveillance programme has been based on the
following considerations:
0  *    meteorological conditions:
*             *    topography of the study area: and
*    likely impact areas.
Location of the ambient air quality monitoring stations were selected on
basis of the wind pattern recorded at IMD, Vadodara over past 30 years
to represent upwind and downwind air quality status with respect to the
road. Locations were selected to represent highest impact area due to
road traffic or to represent background concentrations and for model
calibration.
Ambient Air Quality Monitoring (AAQM) stations were set up at seven
locations for sampling SPM and NOx at eigit' imudy *ierval. . Hourly
observations for NOx, CO and HC during peak t±afic flow period along
with concurrent traffic count were also made to ascertain air quality
levels along the project road at two I n~i- -mdpSa and B--ka
villages. At each station, monitoring was carried out at one location in
the upwind direction and at two locations in the predominant downwind
direction. The predominant wind direction at both locations was decided
based on The results obtained from the meteorological survey carried out
at both locations. AAQM stations were aiso set up at the predominant
downwind locations. The locations of the selected AAQ stations and their
environmental setting is given in the Table-3.5 and Figure-3.8.

3.3.2       Instruments Used for Sampling
High volume samplers of Envirotech instruments were used for
monitoring Total Suspended Particulate Matter (TSPM), and NOx. Mylar
bags with pulse pumps were deployed for collection of eight hourly
samples of Carbon monoxide (CO) and Hydrocarbons (HC). Gas
chromatography techniques have been used for estimation of CO and
HC.
3.3.3      Sampling and Analytical Techniques
1 ) Total Suspended Particulate Matter, and NOx at Monitoring
Stations:
TSPM present in ambient air is sucked through the cyclone. Samples of
gases are drawn at a flow rate of 0.2 liters per minute and are analysed
on the same day of sample collection in the field laboratory located at
Vadodara.
TSPM have been estimated by gravimetai= 4nalbo  Jacobs-Hochheiser
method (IS-5182 Part VI, 1975) has been adopted for estimation of NOx.
Calibration:
Calibration charts have been prepared for all gaseous pollutants. The
calibration is carried out when new absorbing solutions are prepared.
2)    CO and HC at Monitoring Stations:
Mviar bags with pulse pumps    w e   for collection sef -eigt 4thomly
samples of Carbon monoxide (CO). The CO levels were analysed by
Gas-chromatography.
Calibration of Gas Chromatograph:
Calibration has been carried out wjth known gas concentrations and
chromatographs have been taken. The area under the curve
corresponding to the peak of specific gas gives the concentration. The
determined concentration is cross checked with the known value of that
particular gas.
3)    NOx at Continuous Monitoring Station
The continuous NOx instrument operates on a chemiluminescence
technique. When flowing through the converter, the chemiluminescence
measured  within  the  reaction  chamber  represents  the  NOx
concentration. The instrument records readings at every 5 seconds
interval and finally displays 30 minute average.
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
3-19
*;



Environmental Jnpact Assessment                                     Chapter 3
Vadodara - Halol Road                              Baseline Environmental Status
Calibration of Continuous NOx analyzer:
Calibration was undertaken by using precision calibrator 102 NS
manufactured by Thermo Environmental Instruments Inc. USA. The
calibrator has both S02/air cylinder and a NO/NO2 cylinder attachment
to accommodate calibration of two types of gases with one calibrator.
The restructures or capillaries are chosen to provide a dilution ratio of
approximately 100 to 1. Once the dilution ratio is established, a cylinder
of gas is selected with a known concentration when diluted will give a
value of gas necessary to calibrate the analyzer in question to 80% of
the full scale range being used.
A zero air supply capable of delivering upto five liter per minute of zero
air at approximately 10 psi is required. A high concentration of S02/air or
S02/N2 obtained from a compressed cylinder, whose concentration is
determined by the dilution ratio and the value of S02 desired.
NOx:
Sample output of calibrator is connected to NOx analyzer. Calibration is
carried out (1) upon interruption of normal instrument operation for more
than a few days. (2) after any major repair or (3) whenever the span and
zero shift is more than 15% i.e. while passing zero air, the display of the
instrument should be within 0.015 ppm; and while passing NOx of known
concentration, the display of the instrumert stioud be within + 10% of
the known gas concentration in ppm.

3.4         NOISE LEVEL SURVEY
The assessment of impact of noise sources on surrounding community
depends on:
i  *    Characteristics of noise sources (instantaneous, intermittent or
continuous in nature). It can be observed that steady noise is not
as annoying as one which is continuously varying in loudness;
*         The time of day at which noise occurs, for example high noise
levels at night in residential areas are not acceptable because of
sleep disturbance; and
i    The location of the noise source, with respect to noise sensitive
landuse, which determines the loudness and period of exposure.
The environmental impact of noise can have several effects a&yng from
Noise Induced Hearing Loss (NIHL) to annoyance depending on
loudness of noise levels.
The main objective of noise monitoring in the study area is to establish
the baseline noise levels, and assess the impact of the total noise
expected to be generated by the proposed highway construction
activities in the villages around it
Noise survey has been conducted in the study area covering residential,
commercial (transportation) and sensitive zones. Noise monitoring has
been undertaken for 24 hours at each location.
3.4.1       Selection of Sampling Locations
A preliminary reconnaissance survey has been undertaken to identify
the major noise generating sources in the area. Noise at different noise
generating sources have been identified based on the activities like
vehicular traffic in residential and commercial land set up.

3.4.2       Method of Monitoring
Sound pressure level (SPL) measurements were automatically recorded
in the instrument every 10 seconds to give the equivalent noise level for
every hour for 24 hours.
Hourly and 24 hourly values for various noise parameters were tabulated
from above. The day noise levels were estimated for the period 6 am to
9 pm and night levels during 9 pm to 6 am.
Hourly noise levels were also used to estimate the equivalent noise
levels at 15 m and 30 m distance for the four hour peak traffic period at
Anandpura and Baska..
3.4.3       Statistical Analysis of Measurements
' * 4s,       For -noise levels measured over a given period of time interval, it is
possible to describe important features 'i XTie using statistical
quantities. This is calculated using the percent of the time certain noise
levels are exceeded during the time interval. The notation for the
statistical quantities of noise levels are described be4ow:
L,0 is the noise level exceeded 10 per cent of the timre-
L50 is the noise leve4 exceeded 50 per cent of the time, and
L9o is the noise level exceeded 90 per cent of the time.
Equivalent sound Pressure Level (Leq): The Leq is the equivalent
continuous sound level which is equivalent to the same sound energy as
the actual fluctuating sound measured in the same period. I-his is
necessary because sound from noise source often fluctuates widely
during a given period of time. 
This is calculated from the following equation:
(Lt -90)2
Leq =L50 +-
60
Lday is defined as the equivalent noise level measured over a period of
time during day (6 am to 9 pm).
Lnight is defined as the equivalent noise level measured over a period of
time during night (9 pm to 6 am).
A noise rating developed by Environmental Protection Agency, (US-
EPA) for specification of community noise from all the sources is the
Day-Night Sound Level, (Ldn).
Kirloskar Consultants Limited



Environmental Impact Assessment                                       Chapter 3
Vadodara - Halol Road                               Baseline Environmental Status
Ldfl: The noise rating developed for community noise from all sources is
the Day-Night Sound Level(Ldfl). It is similar to a 24 hour equivalent
sound level except that during night time period (9 pm to 6 am) a 10
dB(A) weighting penalty is added to the instantaneous sound level
before computing the 24 hr average.
This night time penalty is added to account for the fact that noise dunng
night when people usually sleep is judged as more annoying than the
same noise during the day time.
The Ldn for a given location in a community may be calculated from the
hourly Leqs, by the following equation.
Ld, = 10 log {1/24[15(10    ) + g9(,o   v0)11
Where Ld is the equivalent sound level during the day time (6 am to 9
pm) and Ln is the equivalent sound level during the night time (9 pm :* 6
am).

residential zone is due to the traffic as well as other village activities.
Commercial zone: The day time noise level at the location (Halol Naka)
prone to traffic and commercial activities is observed to just exceed the
prescribed limit of 65 dB(A). The noise levels recorded at this station
indicates a level of 65.8 dB(A). High noise level is due to traffic and
commercial activities.

underthe residential zone are found to be above the prescribed limits of
45 dB(A). The noise levels at these locations rances between 54.8
dB(A) to 67.4 dB(A). The high noise levels are attributable to the traffic.
Commercial zone: The night time noise level at the location (Halol Naka)
prone to traffic and commercial activities is observed to be 62.7 dB(A)
which exceeds the limits of 55 dB(A).
e)    Pevu a MyfL)

Anandpura and Baska villages falling under the residential zone are
found to exceed the prescribed limits of 55 dB(A).

The purpose of this study is to:
*     assess the water quality characteristics for critical parameters;
*     evaluate  the  impacts  on  agricultural productivity, habitat
conditions, recreational resources and aesthetics in the vicinity;
and

3.5.2      Presentation of Results
Ground and surface water sources were examined for physico-chemical,
heavy metals and bacteriological parameters in order to assess the
effect of domestic and surface runoff from the existing carriageway on
surface and ground water. The samples were collected and analyzed as
per the procedures specified in 'Standard Methods for the Examination
of Water and Waste Water' published by American Public Health
Association (APHA). Samples for chemical analysis were collected in
polyethylene carbuoys. Samples collected for metal content were
-                   acidified with 1 ml HNO3. Samples for Bacteriological analysis were
collected in sterilized glass bottles. Selected physico-chemical and
bacteriological parameters have been analyzed for projecting the
existing water quality status in the study area. Parameters like
temperature, Dissolved Oxygen (DO) and pH are analyzed at the time of
sample collection.
The results for the parameters analyzed for surface and ground water
sampling is presented in detail in Appendix 3.4. The results of S:wLoe
water are compared with Class 'C' water quality (fit for drinking after
n.wanhonal treatment) as per IS:2296-1982 "Tioerance Limits for Inland
Surface Waters subject to Pollution". The ground water results are
compared with standards for drinking water as per IS:10500-1983
'Specifications for Drinking Water".
Surface Wa±arflalWi
The analysis results indicates pH ranges between 7.14 "tn 7.85 while
temperature varies from 280C to 290C. The TDS ranges from 42 to 122
mg/l where as total suspended solids ranges from 124 to 221 mg/l. Total
suspended solids are observed to exceed the standard of 100 mg/I.
BOD levels were observed to be in the range of 15 to 32 mg/I with
dissolved oxygen in the range of 6.9 to 7.8 mg/I. Nitrates (Nil) and
Fluorides (<0.01 mg/I) were observed to be within limits. Total coliform
ranged between 11 to 24 MPN71 00 ml. Heavy metals are observed to be
either absent or below detectable limits. All tested parameters are found
well within the permissible limits as per IS:2296 except for suspended
solids and thereby indicating the good quality of surface water at all
sampling locations.
Ground Water Quality
The analysis results indicates pH at 7.28, TDS at 900 mg/I, Suspended
solids at 65 mg/[, BOO at 12 mg/I, DO at 5.5 mg/I, Nitrates at 24 mg/l and
total hardness at 310 mg/I. TDS is observed to exceed the desirable
standard of 500 mg/I. All other parameters are found to be well within the
permissible limits of IS:10500.

3.7.5       General Observations
Soil colour is observed to be varying between black to slightly black
brown. The texture is observed to be predominantly silty clay followed by
clayey soils. This soil is from trap and is deep retentive and fertile. The
impervious nature of underlying trap at shallow depth makes the soif
extremely retentive of moisture which is found lo be particularly suitabie
to the type of crops grown in this area.
The soils in the area is observed to be slightly to moderately alkaline.
The EC is observed to be average. The Nitrogen levels fall under the
category of "better to sufficient". Similarly, the Potassium levels fall
under the category of "very low to medium'. Whereas, the levels of
Phosphorus flJ undr Mte zategoy of "less to a-age sufficient". The
levels of organic matter in the soils fall under the category of "sufficient
to average sufficient".
3.7.6       Crop Pattern
Agriculture in the area is monsoon dependent and soil is particularly
suited to the dry field crops, such as bajri, tabacco, paddy, cotton, juari,
wheat and vegetables. The main crops grown in the area are cotton,
-      -                 wheat, paddy, groundnut, tobacco, maize, Rabi jowar and tur. Crops
grown on the black soils are cotton and Rabi jowar through paddy is also
grown in fields under irrigation, rotated by wheat in the Rabi season.
The cropping pattem in the western taluks of Panchamahals consists of
mainly cotton, ground nut, jowar, tur and paddy, while the hilly talukas
grow manly maize in the lighter soils and paddy in the low lying areas. A
fairly large acreage is under gram, a crop which is grown as a second
crop after harvesting paddy and maize in the hilly talukas.
The yield per hectare of the principal crops during 1991 to 1992 is given
in Table-3.15.
