-3.8        GEOLOGY, HYDROLOGY AND HYDROGEOLOGY
3.8.1       Geology
Major portion of Vadodara district in westem part is covered by thick pile
of alluvium. The exposures of relatively more complex structured
metasediments of Chamaner series are found some parts of Vadodara.
Lower Champaners ocsur as lenses and contain pebbles of quartzite,
mica schists, limestones, gneisses and granites. The Deccan trap basalt
comprising of lava flows and associated intrusives (dolerite, and alkaline
rocks) which form low lying hills in the northern part of this district and
the westem hill slope of Pavagadh hills. The geological map of
Vadodara Taluka is shown in Figure-3.13 (geology map of the other
talukas were not readily available).
A major portion of Panchamahals district in the central and northern
parts is covered by meta sediments with the extension of the main
Aravalli group of rocks in Rajasthan, while the southem parts are found
with formations which have been referred to as the Champaner series.
These metasedimentary groups of rocks are separated by intrusive
granites and gneisses. In the eastem part of the disirict, Deccan Traps
occupy a large area and at places on their fringes are found in Infra-
trappeans.
The geology of the eastem part of Panchamahals consists of dark
coloured volcanic lava flows and laterites. The major portion is covered
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
:,:'A 'I



Environmental Impact Assessment                                       Chapter 3
Vadodara - Halol Road                               Baseline Environmental Status
by basaltic lava flows known as "Deccan Traps" of upper cretaceous to
lower Eocene in age. The lava flows were poured out of the long and
narrow fissures in the earth's crust and are spread in the form of
horizontal sheets constituting numerous spurs, hills and hill ranges.
broad flat topped ridges, and lofty peaks. One such is near Pavagadh
hills.
The spheroidal weathering of the Deccan trap is very common yielding
soft friable murrum. The recent and sub-recent formations are
represented by laterite, kankar, soil etc. The mechanical and chemical
disintegration of the lava flows have resulted in the formation of laterite
and bauxites. The formation is usually reddish to yellowish in colour and
shows vermicular and tabular cavities often stained with dark brown
ferruginous solution. Laterites are seen on plateau tops. The thickness
of the laterite varies from a few meters to 20 meters.
The mineral resources of commercial quality of Vadodara district mainly
consists of Dolomite, Fourite, Quartz, arble and Oil. Whereas
Panchamahals has a rich treasure of Limestme, Quartz and Marble.
Other main mineral sources such as Bauxite, Fiercely, Lignite, Siderite,
etc. are abundantly available in neighboring dist,
The mineral resources of commercial quality of Vadodara district mainly
consists of Dolomite, Fourite, QuarIz, A MIe  and  Oil. Whereas
Panchamahals has a rich treasure of Limestone, Ouartz and Marble.
Othar ji- a inPjl nm _£& sh -as Bite, Fiem*, Lignite, Sider,te,
etc. are abundantly available in neighboring districts.
Kirloskar Consultants Limited

3.8.2       Hydrology
The Narmada and the Maui are the major rivers in Vadodara district.
Besides these two rivers, the Jambuva, Surva, Vishvamitri and Dhadhar
flow through the district and empty into the Gulf of Cambay. The Unch,
Orsang, Sukhi, Heran, Dev, Karjan, Ashwan and Bhukhi, etc., are
tributaries of the above two major rivers. The river Mahi traverse a
course of about 115 km in this district through Vadodara and Padra
Talukas. Narmada traverses for about 128 km through six talukas in
- *9  =            Vadodara district, viz., Chhota Udepur, Nasadi, Tilakwada, Dabhoi, Sinor
and Karjan.
The Panchmahals district is drained by seven important rivers, viz.,
Mahi, Goma, Kun, Panam, Meshri, Karad and Kali. Many of them take
their rise from the hills of Pavagadh which is about 7 kms away from
i-alol. The Vishvamitri enters Vaghodia taluka and flows past Asoj, Jarod
and Kaamrol villages. The surva river flows past Kotambi, Bhaniyara and
Morlipura villages in Vaghodia talka and passes near Amliyara, Kotali,
and Sukhihpr in Vadodara talka.
There are a number of medium and minor itrigation crossings on the
existing highway road. The main crossings axe at km 32/2 for the
Narmada Main canal of about 110 m top width, and km 10/4 for the
branch canal. As per the figures available for -1986-87, the various
sources of irrigation in Panchamahals are Govemment canals (12400
ha), tanks (1900 ha), weUls (25300 ha). This show that welfs are the main
sources of irrigation.
3.8.3       Hydrogeology
The occurrence, nature and movement of ground water (GW) in the
taluka is entirely dependent upon the topographical setting of the area
and the geological formations encountered. The ground water in rocky
areas is mainly confined to cracks, fissures, joint planes and zones of
weathering. Even the sandstones do not constitute good aquifers since
they are hard, compact and silicious. The basalts do not possess any
primary porosity. The massive traps are hard, compact and
impermeable.
The water levels in rocky terrain varies between 6 m to 15 m below
ground surface, while in alluvial formation the levels vary between 6 m to
27 m. The thickness of the weathering in the area is about 6 m to 15 m.
Out of the total tubewells drilled in selected blocks, about 75.7 % are
successful and 24.3 % have failed either due to poor discharge or
occurrence of hard rock at shallow depth. The yield normally varies
between 15,000 to 40,000 gallons per hour at varying drawdowns
between 9 m and 15 m.
The hydrological tests conducted on tubewells have indicated that the
coefficient of transmissibility of aquifers varies between 78,000 to
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
)7A A



Environmental Impact Assessment                                     Chapter 3
Vadodara - Halol Road                              Baseline Environmental Status
1,30,000 gallons/day/foot and the coefficient of permeability varies
between 680 to 2,000 gallons/day/sq.ft. In general the alluvial aquifers
are capable of transmitting large quantities of groundwater.
In Panchmahals, the hilly and undulating terrain in the east and
comparatively flat terrain in the west are the two regions where
percolation of rainwater and movement of ground water varies markedly.
Adequate supply of water may be obtained in shallow bore wells piercing
the porous horizons of lava flows and in flows traversed by closely
spaced joints. Such wells may yield as-much as 20,000 litres/hour.
From the study of the pre and post monsoon water levels in the
observation wells, which are fixed in all the watersheds depending upon
the morphological units, it is observed that high fluctuations are
observed along the foothills, moderate in the plain.
3.9        '1EMBESTRIAL ECOLOGY
An ecological study of the ecosystem is esseniial to understand the
impact of development activities related to industrialization, urbanization
etc., on existing flora and fauna of the area. The objective of this section
is to characterize and understand the present stars of the ecosystems
in the study area.
The present study was undertaken to understand the present status of
ecosystem along ttie existny tiighway and to compare it with the past
condition with the help of available zl=a, to predict changes as a result of
present activities and to suggest maasures for maintaining the
conditions.
Thus the objectives of ecological study is outlined as follows:
*     to characterize the environmental components like land, water,
flora and fauna; 
--  *  to understand their present status; and
*     to identify susceptible and sensitive areas.
3.9.1      MNethodology of Data Collection
The area adjoining the existing highway (approximately 1-2 km on either
side) has been considered for the primary survey to study the terrestrial
ecosystem. Data from secondary sources have been compiled for the
area. Following methods are being adopted for the ecological study:
* s   Generation of primary data through undertaking systematic
ecological studies along the existing highway and in the study
area;

*     Secondary  data  collected  from  publications  of  various
Govemment agencies like  Forest Department, Agriculture
Department etc.; and
Consulting local people for gathering information on ethnobotany,
local plants and animals.
This section describes terrestrial ecology of the area based on
reconnaissance survey and information gathered from secondary data
available for the area.
3.9.2       Reconnaissance Survey of the Area for Flora
A reconnaissance survey was done to have an overviev. of the terrestrial
ecosystem of the area. The area depicts a manmade ecosystem with
natvral vegetation limited to small patches at certain places. The bio-
diversity of vegetation in the area is not very high due to intense biotic
pressure.
There is no reserved forest, national park, wild life sanctuary or
biosphere reserve in the core area.
3.9.3       Phytosociological Studies
In order to carry our quantitative analysis of terrestrial flora, ecological
sampling was dme  i    the existig 'highway. The phytosociological
studies like relatve dormiance for woody, shrub and   Srlbaeous
species are considered.
Methodology adopted for the study
The most widely used technique of sampling vegetation is that of sample
plots or quadrate. This technique been used for the present project, care
was taken to insure that the sample is adequate to represent the entire
stand. Choice of size, shape, number and distribution of the sample
plots or quadrate changes from the types of plant species and their
distribution. The quadrate sampling technique is applicable for all the
types of plant communities.
The size of the quadrate used for the study was 10 m x 10 m. Smaller
quadrate of 1 m x 1 m was used for smaller plants especially grasses.
The common practice to place major importance on the common
species and count only individuals that are rooted within the plot was
used.
Estimations of, relative density and relative dominance of species were
done as per Mueller-Dombois-Ellenberg. Apart from the above field
sampling, secondary data available for the area has been collected from
the Govemment departments.

3.9.4       Review of Primary Survey Data
The plant life of an area shows certain affinities with the environmental
setting and between the plant species. Normally vegetation analysis is
directed towards the natural plant communities as they relate to the
various site factors. Disturbed areas often have fragmented plant
associations having no community structure or integrity. But in either
case, the analysis considers the dominant species that characterize the
site, -the sensitivity of the vegetation to impact, and presence of rare and
endangered species.
The vegetation along the highway course shows sparse density of
vegetation. A sample survey of the area was carried all along the
highway.
Plant Survey:
'Plants have been assessed in several descriptive ways:
a)    A "species list" includes both local and scientific names of the
plants found in the study area. This list is made by site visits and
by consulting published literature. Secondary sources are
particularly important for out of season annual species or for
pssible rare or endangered plant species Such wurces have
been referenced.
b)   TJbe "a"be of plants" proves information on the  irpvition,
vegetation pattems, and relatiie dabwdance of each species in
the area. Such information has allowed comparison to be made
between stands of the same type or with other vegetation stands
of different types in order to establish commonness or rarity of
species of vegetation types on or near the study sites.
3.9.5       Quantitative Plant Assessment
Since plants occur in recognizable associations and interact with one
another, special quantitative methods are used in assessing the
vegetative parameters. Beyond considering the species composition of a
vegetation, these methods provide information on the role and relative
importance of individual plant species (such as dominance and rarity),
relationship between plants, species distribution and spacing, number of
individuals of each species, and structure of plant community.
Quantitatively the vegetation can be analysed in several ways, out of
which 'sample plot' method was selected for the present project.
Trees of Mangifera indica and Madhuca latifolia are found dotting the
agricultural lands and Acacia nilotica trees occupy bunds of some fields.
Species such as Lantana camara, Prosopis juliflora, Impomea SP,
Cassia tora, Xanthium strumarium, Parthenium hysterophorus etc. are

3.9.5.1    Vegetation
The vegetation is influenced by dry and hot summer which sets in April
and lasts till June. April and May the hottest months. Monsoon rainfall
is restricted to June to October, July and August being wettest months.
Winter starts in November and lasts till February. Rainfall ranges from
600 mm to nearly 1500 mm, a-verage being around 1000 mm. The
temperature varies from 140C to 46 C.
All these factors define the nature of vegetation and ecosystem in this
area in general.
The Vadodara-Halol road passes through plains which are
characterised by fertile agricultural fields and human settlements. The
^   _              ae systlem  is under considerabie numan interference especially
grazing, agriculture, lopping for firewood and fee and urbanisation.
Barring human interference, the natural composi¶in of vegetation in
such an ecosystem would be dominated by Acia nilotica, Prosopis
spicigera, Capparis decidua, Butea monosperma, Borassus flabellifer,
Phoenix  sylvestris,  Zizyphus  spp.,  Pitheembbnjm   duke  and
occasionally occuring Mangifera indica. Shruby vegetation would be
dominated by Caotfropis gigantea and Cassia occidentaIis. But the
intensive agriculture has changed the characieristics of vegetatJon and
is now dominated by weeds, exotoc species esp. omameriSaLs and
economically important plants restricted to field bunds.
The economically important plants like Teak and Ain which should be
forming an important component of this ecosystem are missing, but
few cultivated patches of Teak.
After through survey of this area the ecosystem can be categorized
into 5 different types of communities.
a)    Roadside vegetation
b)    Ravines and streambed vegetation
c)    Field bund vegetation
d)    Plantations on private lands
e)    Vegetation around human settlements.

a)    Roadside Vegetation
Along the sides of existing Vadodara-Halol road, very old avenue trees
like Ficus bengalensis. Ficus retusa, Azadirachta indica and
Tamarindus indica are seen. Most of these are large trees above 200
cm GBH. It has been on record that these trees were planted by
Maharaja of Baroda State His Highness Sayajirao Gaikwad. As we
move from Baroda to Halol, first half is dominated by Azadirachta
indica i.e. Neem or Lindo and laterhalf by two species of Ficus - F.
retusa and F bengalensis. Babul i.e. Acacia nilolica, Shami i.e.
Pnosopis spicigera are common associates. Pithecolobium has been
first planted and then naturalised is found abundant between 29th and
31st km stretch. An omamental large tree Peltophoram which has
recently been planted is observed in thick clusters in km 2,3 and 27
and 28. Similarly Delonix is also found in 4th and 27th km stretch. A
p-n,nent row of 24 full grown Ashoka trees - Polyalthia longifolia is
observed at 28' km stretch.
Along the sides of roads and nearby field bunds Lawsonia, lpomesea
fistulosa and at few places Euphorbia and dominates. In
several stretches, a noxious shruby weed, Prosopis juliflora forms
significant patches. All these shrubs are not palafible and hence not
grazed by cattle and form a good natural fence. On degraded open
lands especially at the Ist stretch prosopis julifiora has become a
dominant weed and is spreading fast.
b)   Ravinues and Streambed Vegetation
Degraded lands, waste lands, ravines, streambeds show mainly,
growth of Prosopis juliflora esp. between 30 and 31St km stretch. Along
with Prosopis, Cassia occidentalis, Zizyphus spp. and Capparis are
observed.
c)    Field Bund Vegetation
Protected field bunds mainly have Acacia Prosopis spicigera, Butea
monosperma and Mangifera indica as natural vegetation. In addition to
this, people have planted Enealyptus. Cassias, Banhinia and Mango.
Between km stetches 19 and 20 and 25 to 28 well grown groves of
Borassus - a palm, are found. Most of these palm trees show
association with Ficus species.
d)    Plantations of Private Lands
At few places, agroforestry activity is seen. Recently 3-4 years back, at
8 km stretch and 13 and 14h km stretch Eucalyptus has been planted.
the plantation is close spaced with average height of 5 cm.

Near Panchdeola village, 15-20 year old teak plantation is observed.

village, Eucalyptus, Gaura, Mango, Spathodia, Cassias, Polyalthia,
have been commonly planted. At Basaka village at 28 km a new

e)    Vegetation Around Human Settlements
In the open spaces of villages and village roads Prosopis juliffora,
Cassia glanca, Cassia siamea, Bauhinia, Enealyptus, Spathodia,
Loueeana (subabul), Peltophorum, Parkinsonia, Pithe colobium and
Neem are observed. Most of the trees of ornamented value like
Spathodia, Cassias, Peltophorum and Neem have been planted. The
village wastelands show a fairly good cover of Proposisjuliflora.

present status of vegetation along the Vadodara - Halol road. All the .
trees above 30 cm GBH and those fall within proposed aqisition have

deformed and stunted even after protection and proper tree care. The
trees which are damaged were Neem and Ficsv -lnce both can be used
as a cattle or goat fodder in scarcity during summer.
3.9.6       Secondary Data
The data available from secondary sources is also presented in
Appendix 3.5. Since the primary survey is site-specific, it is of more
relevance than the secondary data.
3.9.7      Summary Remarks on Vegetation
This region represents a completely modified dry deciduous forest
ecosystem into typical rainfed agro ecosystem. Within a range of 20
km on eitherside of road there doesn't exist any natural forest. Nearest
protected forest is at Pavagadh range at about 5 km from Halol.
Vegetation is mainly restricted to roadsides and field bunds, the trees
are either economical important species protected on field bunds like
mango, teak or terminalia or weeds like thorny acacias and prosopis.
Most of the Avian fauna that is found is associated with agriculture and
human habitation, except few common migratory birds which roost
enroute of their winter migration. Otherwise birds fauna is dominated
by grainivores, insectiones and ommirores. Raptors' density is less.
Since there is no significant forests around, the trees and shrubs on
field bunds and roadsides provide important roosting, brooding and
Derchina sites. The tress Ficus SDD. and Neem on Vadodara - Halol



Environmental Impact Assessment                                   Chapter 3
Vadodara - Halol Road                             Baseline Environmental Status
road provide important feeding and roosting sites for birds especially
when no food is available elsewhere in summer.
Being an highly disturbed ecosystem area does not harbour any
residential large mammals except common squirrels, hares and few
foxes. And since there are no large patches of protected forests on
either side of road, any road development activity will not cause loss
any corridors/bridges of migration of animals. Ecosystem is being
rapidly invaded by a noxious weed Prosopis juliflora which is altering
this ecosystem rapidly.
Protection of Existing Avenue:
Mostly the Neem and species of Ficus form well grown avenues of
existing Vadodara-Halol road. These trees play a significant role,
especially during scorching summer months, in lives of local people,
travellers and even birds. Efforts should be taken to protected existing
wefl grown trees, as much as possible, when existing carriage width is
incmased.
3.9.8      Terrestrial Fauna
There is no suitable habitat for occurrence ecologically important fauna
in the wea. The list of wild and domestic animals, and avifauna sighted
in the area are given in Appendix 3.5. No endangered species have
been reported in The study area. The mammalian fauna is limited and
does not show much diversity. They mainly constitute ftmeslated
animals and rodents. The area hapbo-rs grainivorous avian fauna
because agricultural grains in the field provide food for such birds (See
Appendix 3.5 for the list). Amphibian and reptilian fauna is poor and they
are represented by toads, lizards and snakes.
Since this project does not pass through ecologically sensitive area, the
abundance of the fauna is of less sigpficance.
3.10       AQUATIC ECOLOGY
The important surface water bodies like ponds, irrigation canals situated
albng the existing highway were identified for field sampling. These
surface water bodies may exhibit ecological changes due to the
proposed construction activities.

To assess the baseline conditions with respect to phytoplankton and
zooplankton, four locations were selected for sampling. The details of
the sampling locations are brought out below:
Khandewadi Village (Pond) : Right side of the road at a distance
of 20 m from the edge of the road;

3.12         Socio-Economic Aspects
The brief account of social assessment study is dealt in Annexure - I
with respect to social assessment study process, land acquisition and
SE                      Resettlement & Rehabilitation (R&R) policy leading to preparation of
Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) for the project.
This section describes about the socio-economic aspects of project
villages in the project area. Vadodara - Halol Road Project (VHRP)
covers two districts - Vadodara and Panchamahal. Large section of
Vadodara - Halol road passes through the district of Vadodara. Of the
proposed 31.5 Kms road, 25.9 Kms passes through Vadodara district
and the remaining 5.6 Kms comes in Panchamahal district.

3.12.1      Methodology Adopted For The Study
The methodology adopted for the study is based on the following:
Villages on the either side of the existing alignment have been
considered for socio-economic studies. According to 1991
census, there are 14 villages in the study area. All these villages
form a part of the rural area despite being very near to the urban
area.
;  .=*  Review of secondary data was done for the above 14 villages
from 1991 and 1981 District Census, Gazetteers, District
Statistical Hand-books. Urban development plans with respect to
population, occupational structure and infrastructure facilities
available in the region.

compiled from secondary sources. The demographic data has mainly
been compiled from Census Handbook, 1991. The car_in qjcal aspects
of this study include human settlements, demographic and other socio-
economic aspects and infrastructural facilities available in the study
area. The economic aspects include agriculture, industry and
occupational structure of workers. The socio-economic details are briefly
described in the following sections.

4.0        IMPACT ASSESSMENT
-41         INTRODUCTION,,.
-The present chapter identifies and appraises various impacts from the
proposed project based on -environmental baseline conditions and
analysis of the field data as discussed in the earlier chapter.
The environmental impacts are categorized as either primary (direct) or
secondary (indirect). Primary impacts are those which are attributed
-directly by the project whereas secondary impacts are those which are
indirectly induced and typically include the associated investment and
[S    -changing patterns of social and economic activities due to the proposed
action. Typically, the potential direct and indirect impacts of such
thighway pfbjects are summarized in Table-4.1.

Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Impact Assessment
The air quality predictions in the present application have been carried   '  -'
out by using the air quality-model CALINE3 developed by Califomia
Department of Transportation. The model is based on Gaussian
diffusion, equation and uses: a mixing zone concept to characterise
pollutant dispersion over the toadway. The model has been extensively
tested for its predictive capability for traffic related air quality impacts.
Given  source -strength, meteorology, site  geometry  and  site
characteristics the model can reliably predict pollutant concentrations
for receptors located within 150 meters of the roadway, the most
important region for estimating the impacts of road project due to the
low elevation emissions.
The seasonal dependence of model outputs, primarily due the
r-Xt                         variations. in meteorological conditions over the year, has been'
C]      'accounted for by developing the predictive scenarios for the winter
months during which the air quality is poorest due to low dispersion of
the pollutants.
The long term variations in air quality scenarios during the project life
f!          '        '' '    are expected due to the change in traffic on the project road with time.
L..                          The traffic volumes for the year 2000 and 2015, respectively, have
been considered to project future air quality scenarios to provide an
indication of long term variations in air quality. A longer time horizon
has not been considered because -of uncertainty in ascertaining the
emission factors for various categories of vehicles beyond the year
2015 due to the probable change in technology and fuel use.
To account for the effect of the diurnal variations in model inputs
(vehicular emissions and meteorological conditions), the averaging
time for model predictions has been restricted to 60 minutes. The
averaging time is so' selected because the primary meteorological
factors that inflience the air quality predictions i.e. wind speeds and
directions do not remain steady for longer time periods. Also, during
the peak  traffic hours, traffic volumes typically show significant
variations over periods longer than one hour.
Due to averaging time of 60 minutes, the project impacts on air quality
are essentially assessed based on one hourly standard for CO. CO
standard for 8 hourly average concentration has also-been compared
with the predicted levels by averaging the hourly estimates of predicted
CO levels for eight hours (4 prn to 12 pm) which are critical due to- 
higher traffic volumes, lower wind velocities and adverse dispersion
conditions.
L
Due to non-availability of hourly variations of meteorological conditions
over 24 hours, daily average levels could not be predicted for'
i:-   comparison with the relevant standards of NOx and SPM. Further
scale up factors for estimating 24 hourly average concentrations for 1
I :     -  - -- ---- ------ - -  -  -  '''  Klrloskar Consultants Umited
,r,L-                                                                                             :: :.

Vadodara - Halol Road                                   Impact Assessment
-hour.are not reported. In any case such an exercise requires that the
wind is blowing in the same direction for all 24 hours - which is not a
practical assumption. NOx levels however have been developed for
peak traffic conditions and compared with WHO standlard of 400 gg/m3
for hourly average. -
HC and SPM, although contributed by the vehicular mnovement, were
- not modelled. For HC, the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986 does
not specify the ambient standards and therefore it would have not
been possible to interpret the modelling results meaningfully. In case of
SPM, vehicle SPM emission levels have not been specified by the
Indian Institute of Petroleum. Also, SPM contributions are dominated
by background concentrations as well as traffic induced resuspension,
both of which are difficult to quantify.,
;Carbon Monoxide Levels:
The source emission levels for CO were estimated by using the peak
hourly traffic volumes for vehicles of five categories i.e., trucks and
.-buses, LCVs, cars, two wheelers and three wheelers. The traffic
volumes for-these categories of vehicles were obtained by distributing
'the total daily predicted traffic volumes for respective vehicle types for
the years 2000 and 2015 over the 24 hours according to the hourly
proportions observed during. the traffic count in 1996, Thus, the ratio
factors of hpurly traffic to average daily traffic for each categories of
-vehicles obtained for the year 1996 have been used for the years 2000
and 2105 to estimate -the corresponding projected hourly traffic of the
respective vehicle types by considering the average traffic in the
respective years. The ratio factors estimated for trucks were assumed
to be applicable for and similarly of cars to two and three wheelers
-because of their nature. The Table-4.3 presents the estimated counts
:. - . F . -  V  -  ---from 4 pm to -11 pm, the eight hourly period used for developing 8
hoyrly averages for CO.

NOxLevels:
Similar to emission factors for CO, values for NOx were obtained from
the standards proposed by IIP and corrected for the variations in speed
using WHO' guidelines. The emission factors thus derived are also
presented in Table-4.4. One hourly simulations for NlOx were carried
out on Ahe lines similar to CO. The adverse stability classes were
. -considered for both wind cases in -the view of the maximum
D ffi -, tS ^            --- --  7 - concentration predicted for the particular stability classes. Thus,
' '14    a      0   Xt i stability classes E and F were considered for parallel and cross wind
cases respectively.
~~~~~~~....                       *---- -     . --.---   -
The results for 1 hour average values for NOx'for the years 2000 and
-201 5 are presented in Figure-4.3 and 4.4 respectively. When
compared toWHO standard of 400 ng/m3, it :is observed that under
cross wind conditions the standard is expected to get violated up to a s
distance -of about 35m from the ROW limit only in thie year 2015 for
'With Project scenario.-The prediction for 'Do Nothing' scenario were
observed to be lower than that of 'With Project' scenario in both the
years of prediction. No violations of NOx standards are! expected in the
year 2000.     - - -'
Due to unavailability of data on Xmeteorological conditions. 24 hourly
-average simulations have not been possible. Regions of probable.
violation of Indian Standard of 80 pg/m3 for 24 hour average levels
therefore could not be stated. Considering the fluctuation in the wind
,-  ,-; -. :X   ,,-directions over a period of 24 =hours during the -winter, however, it is
safe to assume that such violations will -not be more than the violations
of 1 hourly average standard of 400 pg/M3.
When viewed with respect to the short term -standard for NOx, on an
average violation cis 12-15 %. The traffic ;'figures used in predicting are *
-estimated peak hourly traffic which lasts for about 1-4 hours.

4.4.2      Impact dn Noise Levels
COnstruction Phase
During the construction, the major sources of noise pollution are
- tmovement of vehicles transporting the construction material to the,
construction yard and the noise generating activities at the yard itself.
Concreting, mixing, casting and material movement are primary noise
generating activities in the yard and will be uniformly distributed over the
entire construction period. Construction activities are expected to
- -  SZ      -produce noise levels in the range of 80 - 95 dB(A). The major work will
be carried out during the day time. The noise produced during the
construction will however not have a significant impact on the existing
ambient noise levels near villages (where the existing ambient noise
levels for residential zone are already being exceeded) because:  -
the proposed alignment bypasses the four villages as
discussed in Chapter 2; and
the predominant land use along  the alignment is
agricultural and open fields due to which lthere will be very
limited presence of population being exposed to noise
levels.
The construction equipment will have high noise levels which can affect
the personnel operating the machines. Use of proper personal protective
equipment will mitigate any adverse impact of the noise generated by
such equipment.
The noise levels in the working environment are compared with the
- -- - : :4   ~             q i  standards prescribed by Occupational Safety and Health Administration
(OSHA-USA) which in-tum are being enforced by Govemment of India
through Model rules framed under the Factories Act. The acceptable
limits for each shift being of 8 hour duration, the equivalent noise level
exposure during the shift is 90 dB(A). Hence noise generated due to
various activities in the construction camps may affect workers, if
equivalent 8, hour exposure is more than the safety limit. ACGIH
* (American Conference of Govemment Industrial Hygienists) proposed
- ~. - an 8 hour L4 limit of 85 dB(A). Exposure to impulses or impact noise
should not exceed 140 dB(A) (Peak acoustic pressure). Exposure to
10,000 impulses of 120 dB(A) are permissible per day.
The noise lkely to be generated during excavation, loading and
-- {i- - ;transportation of material near the borrow areas will be in the range of 90
to 105 dB(A)1and Shis -will-occur only when all the equipments operate
W-;                        i ~--Iogether and- simultaneously. This will be a remote possibility. The
workers in general are likely to be exposed to an equivallent noise level
- I-, X i                        of 80-90 dB(A)'in an 8 hour shift for which all statutory precautions as .
per 'B' laws should be taken-into consideration.

--However these levels can be reduced by careful planning of machinery
operations and scheduling of operations.
_%                             *     Operational Phase
Uninterrupted movement of heavy and light vehicles at high speeds will
have give rise to increase in ambient noise levels along the roadway. It
may have negative environmental impacts on the sensitive receptors
located within the zone of influence. In order tQ estimate the noise
L                              impacts with respect to existing situations, noise monitoring was
undertaken at Dhanjipur and Jarod, two roadside villages in the project.
It was observed that the observed day time and night time equivalent
i                              noise levels were more than the prescribed permissible limits at the
villages monitored for noise levels.
At these villages, the day noise levels ranged between 62.9 dB(A) to
65.6 dB(A) with the night noise levels in the range of 54.8 dB(A) to 67.4
dB(A).
L                             -With the operation of the proposed road the residential areas along the
highway on either side are likely to experience high day and night time
noise levels. The impact of noise levels from the existing and the
L.                            proposed highway on the neighboring communities is addressed by
carrying out computations using Highway Noise Model developed
based on the guidelines suggested by Federal Highway Administration
(FHWA). The details of the model and the model computations are
described below.-
4.4.2.1    Noise Modeling
l  *    Detailsof Noise Model -
L                 '
The Sound-Pressure Level (SPL) generated by noise sources decreases
with increasing distance from the source due to wave divergence. An
additior-.3 decrease in SPL with distance from the source is expected
.due to atmospheric effect or its interaction with objects in the
transmission path.
Estimating highway noise impact involves complex process. which will
require a large computer analysis to model in detail. However, the
methodology presented below is a general highway noise model, that
predicts the equivalent noise level (Le) and is adequate for most noise
assessment requirements. The advantage of the model which predicts
Leq is that Lq is the "Energy Average' noise level and as such is not
dependent on the statistics .of the traffic flow. In contrast, the noise
descriptors LIo, L50, Lgo can be very sensitive to the flow characteristics
r;                           of the traffic. Further, the model presented below can be applied equally
well to high and low traffic volume roadways.
,The Highway Noise Model presented below is for calculating the one
hour Leq. The model is based upon calculating the hourly Liq for

The model results reveal that L,1 levels are observed to be exceeding    '
the standard prescribed for residential zone in both the scenarios. And
1Lnight values for 'With Project' Scenario are predicted higher even than
L                               Lt.ght values of 'Do Nothing' scenario in both the years.
'The existing noise levels at some locations are monitored to be
exceeding the Ltay and Lnight standards. In the background of this
prevailing high background noise, the noise levels are predicted higher
even without the project. Since most of the villages along the highway
IL               -are situated within a reach of about 150 m from the existing road. Lday
standard for residential zone is violated upto a distance of about 150 m
.-and 250 m from the edge of ROW in 2000 and 2015 A.D. respectively
during 'Do Nothing scenario'. Whereas L ght standard for residential
-zone is violated upto a distance of about 700 m. from ROW in both the    I
modeled years. Lay -violations are not expected to occur in the    F. -
-commercial zone in both the modeled years. Since there will be no
commercial activity at night, Light violations for commercial zone are
not considered.
The proposed project is expected to violate 14,y standard for residential
zone upto a distance of about 300 m and 700 m in 2000 and 2015 A.D.
respectively. Whereas Lffi standard for residential zone is violated
upto a distance of about 700 m in both the modeled years. I-day
violations are. expected lo occur in the commercial zone upto a
-distance of about 45 m and 125 -m from ROW in 2000 and 2015 A.D.
respectively. However, with the provision of bypasses across two major
residential areas viz, Jarod and Asoj, noise levels are less likely to
violate the respective standards. If any new land developments are
restricted only beyond 45 .m .from centre of the road, the noise
L  -      violations would not be all that significant.
The school existing at a distance of about 100 m from ROW boundary
at village Jarod -is classified under a 'Silence Zone'. During 'Do
Nothing' scdnario, .it is likely -to experience day noise levels (Liy) of
56.8 dB(A) and .58.5 .dB(A) in 2000 and 2015 A.D. respectively.
Whereas during 'With Project' scenario, the Lday noise levels at school
would be 61.3 .dB(A). and 66 dB(A) in 2000 and 2015 A.D. respectively
(against Lday standard for silence zone of 50 dB(A)). The noise levels
would be attenuated by 5 - 8 dB(A) with boundary wall raised to 3 m
and provision of landscaping..
The computations carried out gives an indication of noise levels in an
worst case scenario, i.e. without taking into consideration the local
features like landuse, barriers etc. In reality, the noise levels are
expected to be on lower side due to these considerations. Moreover the
F                             noise levels monitored in the villages viz., Jarod, Halol naka, Dhanjipura
have recorded high levels in the range of 62.9 dB(A) to 65.6 dB(A)
during day and 54.8 dB(A) to 67.4 dB(A) during night. This is mainly due*
to local activities and high background levels.

4.4.3      Impact on Water Quality,
'The present water quality has been assessed by studying four surface: .
waters and one ground water samples. The results are presented in-
Appendix 3.4. The summary of the same is presented below:
Impact on Surface Water Quality                        t i
All tested parameters are found well within the permissible limits as per,1
;!' ,         -                -      IS:2296 and thereby indicating-the good quality of surface water at all'-
sampling locations.
The construction and operation of the proposed highway project will not
''have any-.major impact on the surface water and the ground water
quality in Ahe area. Contamination to water bodies may result due to:
spilling of construction materials, oil, grease, fuel and paint in the;
equipment'yards and asphalt plants. But, the quantities of such spills is
very negligible.
Care, however needs to be taken to provide adequate sanitary facilities
and drainage in the temporary colonies of the construction workers.
Provision of adequate washing and toilet facilities with septic tanks and
appropriate refuse collection and disposal system  ishould be made
obligatory-.
'The water-quality -of the existing surface water bodies vfiz, ponds, rivers,
irrigation canals are observed to be potable. The ponds at villages Asoj,
Khandewadi and Baska are basically used by the nearby habitants for
domestic purposes. During construction care should be taken not to alter
the odginal water quality status of the ponds. This can be achieved by
2- 1-;1 ' i       - ---  X  0- ' 0 ;;' excluding these ponds as a source for water during construction -
'-w                             ' (including that for the construction camps) providing water and sanitary
facilities in the construction camps etc.
An adequate foresight and creation of'physical facilities; will minimize the
above problems. This should form an integral component in the planning
stage before commencement of construction activity.
Impact on Ground Water Quality
Ground water pollution can take place only if a dump contains chemical
substances,- which will get leached by precipitation of water and
percolate to the ground water table. This is not the case with the present
project as the activity does not contain any harmful ingredients which
-  .: g  -  - -.,, S  .. - :   -could leach-down to water table. Hence impact on tlhe ground water
. "'' E,             r ,s- *quality is not anticipated from the-project during the construction and
-ioperational phase    -  -

4.5        IMPACT ON ECOLOGICAL RESOURCES
The baseline flora and fauna has been depicted in Section 3.9, Chapter-
;L  ~   ^ #  - 3. Accordingly, there is no wildlife sanctuary, reserved or protected forest
in the close vicinity of the proposed highway. The area in a radius of 50
-'- --        -            - ~km hosts small patches of forest land, which mainly consists of few
scrubs and trees. There are no endangered or rare species in the forest
patches. The ecological impacts are briefly described in the following
sections.
4.5.1      Impact on Terrestrial Ecology
The initial construction works at the project site involving land clearance,
cutting, filling and leveling, will cause loss of potential agricultural
productive land and loss of vegetation. A total of 966 trees out of 1621
(identified along the road - see Table -3.15) are required to be cut down
for the construction work.
Li 8 -  -    -         -    Inevitably, there will be a short term impact of availability of nesting sites
for the birds. -Since the area harbors mainly local grainivourous avian
fauna, this should not affect their nesting requirements. Further, there is
no sensitive ecological area near the existing road, so the impact will not
be of significance. With the landscaping after the project, the number of
trees are expected to be more than earlier numbers and will allow the
L:                          short term impact to be reversed.
Li
The ROW  requirements and the borrow areas does not involve any
reserved/protected forest land and therefore project impacts in the forrn
i .                          . - - - of loss of forest cover do not exist. ;;
[l .' -- The barren / grassland areas covered by the alignment have little or no
tree growth.                           ;--

fcj.                        top soil generally causes soil erosion. However, such impacts would be
primarly confined to the,project.site during initial periods of the
construction phase and, would be mirinimized through adoption of
L:         -  - -      .   mitigative measures like paving and. surface treatment, water sprinkling
and appropriate plantation program. The project site will be landscaped
with the development of green belt consisting of a variety of trees which
would enrich the ecology of the area and add to the aesthetics.
-    -- -                  The constructional activities lead to inward migration of labour force in
the area and thus there would be pressure on trees in the area due to
increase in fuel demand. ln order to prevent felling of trees in the
neighboring areas, adequate alternate fuel should be arranged to meet
the fuel requirement of labour.force.   --

Wild Life
No rare or endangered species have been reported from the area
covered by the proposed alignment. No wild life travel routes of t
significance'have been noted/recorded along the alignment. The wild life
in these area do not confer any significant benefit on the local
population.
The increased vehicular traffic coupled with higher noise levels due to
various constructional activities may drive away the local fauna from the
project site to the neighboring area. As the project is devoid of forest and
trees, it does not harbor any fauna of importance. Therefore, the impact
of constructional activities on fauna will be negligible.
4.5.2       Impact on Aquatic Ecology
The -proposed road would pass over three rivers viz. Viswamitr,
Narmada Canal and river Surva. River overbrdges aire planned over -
these rivers adjoining the existing bridges. No signifiicant impacts on
aquatic ecology of the area are perceived due to the construction of
-  f   ; --    t    these bridges.
4.6         IMPACT ON HUMAN USE VALUES
4.6.1       Soils
The impact on soil due to this project is in terms of top soil erosion.
Considerable care should be taken while locating the camps buildings,
borrow pits, quarries, spoil and disposal site which will minimize the soil
erosion. The impact of construction would be minor as a very small area
is susceptible to excessive soil erosion. Soil pollution would take place to
-a negligible extent due to spillage of construction material, oil, fuel,
'4            L        ~         'grease and-asphalt around the construction yards.
:4.6.2  ;    Land Use Development
,The.-development bin the study area will definitely bring substantial
changes in the-landuse pattem. In this process, areas presently under
agriculture and vegetal cover will be diverted for development and for
other usagei The acquisition of less fertile fallow lands mostly covered
4: -A.i  0 0.  j -        -  ' - -- iunderthe revenue'land and its conversion for different dlevelopments will
lead to enriched land uses which include construction of residential area,
offices and other infrastructural facilities and these activities may occur
in the immediate vicinity of the proposed.project area.
- The proposed project includes landscaping tree plantation all along the
road alignment which will add to the quality of life and aesthetics of the
area. Diversion of road sections away from the  inhabited areas in
major enroute towns and villages will also lead to improved air quality
and noise levels.

4.7        OTHER ISSUES
4.7.1      Transport of Hazardous Material
As brought out in Chapter 2, during the road side sunrey, it was found
that about 23.9% of the commodities transported through the existing
road are of hazardous type comprising of petroleum products (0.6%),
industrial raw materials (95%), petrol and oil (7.0%) and chemicals and
fertilizers (6.8%).
After the i(nplementation of the proposed road, increase in traffic is
envisaged due to development of industries and other allied activities in
the close vicinity. On the other side, congestion problems and probability
of accidents will reduce considerably due to the better design service
Jevel.
'The spillage materials on the road near the ponds or rivers/canals may
cause loss of life or property as also adverse water quality impacts, if the
materials find their way into -surface water bodies. 1ransport of the
hazardous materials by road is regulated by Motor Vehicle Act 1989,
which provides precautions to be followed by the consignor, owner of the
.goods carrier and its driver to minimize the risk of accidents and damage
control in the event of mishap. As a precautionary measure, the nearest
fire brigade stations should be upgraded adequatelyt to handle the
emergencies arising from accidents involving spillage, of hazardous
materials.  -
4.7.2      Issues Related to Proposed Alignment
-A-review of the proposed alignment at Jarod, Asoj, Khandewadi and
Baska villages indicates the recommended alignment will bypass the
villages (at Asoj options of passing only the new 2 lane carriageway
outside the village and retaining the existing carriageway or having a
new carriagewayoutside            Thesealgnmetsaave
new 4 lane carriageway outside the village exists, but in any case the
* 0 '' 'tj:'' ... w. - ,,, Snew camrageway~ passes outside-the village). These alignments have 
been recommended-to minimize the social and environmental impacts
by ruling out other options which would have othervise resulted in the

relocation of families, permanent and temporary structures and
business, trees, place of worship and encroachment of surface water
bodies.
The proposed alignment -at Khandewadi occupies about 10% of the
pond area (about 15 m wide and about 100 m length), which is located
just adjacent to the existing carriageway. The village is situated on the
opposite side of the pond. Pedestrian and cattle crossing the existing
carriageway from the village for using the pond is observed. A survey of
utilization of pond for various-uses has been carried out and the findings
of the same is presented in Table-2.5 (chapter 2).
A perusal of the data presented in the above table reveals that on an
average about 190 persons use the pond facility daily for various
purposes. About 42.6% of them use pond for bathing, 16.8% for cloth
washing and the remaining (40.6%) use for other- purposes. The data
shows that the local in-habitants are dependent on the pond facility.
The land acquisition -is likely to limit the holding capacity of the pond,
which may lead to flooding of adjacent areas during monsoon season.
This impact can be minimized by resizing the ROW requirements in the
pond area, and implementation of mitigatory measures like deepening of
the pond, bank 'improvement works or provision of altemative water
supply by borewells to the local in-habitation near the village, which will
further reduce dependence of villagers on the existing pond and reduce
pedestrian crossing of the road.
However, it is important to note that the areas to be reclaimed from the
Vi    -ponds do not lie on their perphery and includes only those area which
remain dry except for the monsoon. The reclamation, therefore, would
not have any significant impact on the ecology of the ponds, once,, the
holding capacity of the ponds is restored.

4.7.4      Cross Drainage Works
The existing alignment has 17 RCC pipe drains, 12 RCC slab drains, 3
minor bridges and 3 major bridges. The existing waterways are
functioning normally apart from local scouring near the unprotected -
foundations. Even though the waterways are blockedl by vegetation at
many locations, there is no record of flooding or overtopping of
embankment.
The proposed project envisages repair/reconstruction of the existing CD
works and bridges where warranted and construction of new bridges and
CD works Qadjacent to the existing ones (due to widening of the
carriageway). Hence impact of CD works is not considered to be of
significance.

Environ7mental and SocialAssessment Report                      Chapter 5
Vadodara - Halol Road                                    Project Altematives
:5.0       'PROJECT AND NO;PROJECT SCENARIOS                               -
L<,. :: ~. :.  a. . :-    5.1     BACKGROUND
The Vadodara - Halol road which links Vadodara city to the industrial
-town of Halol has been identified as one of the most important State
. - r . 3 T         ;      -    Highways in the State of Gujarat which require immediate upgradation
by the strategic options study of the state road network carried out by
the Govemment of Gujarat (GoG) in 1995. The need for the project is
summarised in Chapter 2. The main objective of the proposed
Vadodara-Halol highway project is to provide a faster and reliable route
to cater to the heavy traffic on the existing highway by providing an
additional carriage way that upgrades the link to a four lane divided
carriage -way from  the present two lane individual link. As the
Vadodara-Halol road is a imminent link and the traffic is expected to
grow at a fast rate, the proposed project is a key to improved traffic
management in the region.
The present highway passes through villages, has substandard curves
which requires improvement at 35 locations (out of 43) to achieve a
higher level of service, experiences traffic congestion and lack of safety
due to which the air and noise pollution as well as accidents are on the
rise. The engineering designs evolved out widening of the carriageway   .'
and improvement of geometrics resulted in various altemative
F.                            - alignments. These altematives were assessed based on social,  ,.:
-,,,environmental -and engineering considerations. The recommended   -
alignment which minimis&s the social and environmental impacts (land   :
L :-X-    :    i .0   -:       .acquisition,. R&R -issues, pond encroachment, increased safety,   [
reduced noise and air pollution) is recommended (Chapter 2 provides
the details).      : 
It is on this background that In the present chapter, two scenarios viz.,
the "No Project" and "This Project' scenarios have been discussed.
5-   ^  :.2  NO PROJECT SCENARIO
The present Vadodara-Halol highway is heavily loaded with traffic.
A three day average traffic volume counts carried out at two locations
viz., one near Vadodara and, the second near Halol in early 1996
indicates that nearly 50% of vehicles are goods vehicles.
Classified traffic volume count survey conducted for seven days
conducted at two locations on the existing project road indicates that
goods vehicles constitute nearly 45% of the traffic. Car traffic is about
L;                         *   20%, whereas buses constitute a mere 6%.                            - -
The seasonal variation in traffic flow studied using the past 10 years*
month wise toll collection revenues indicates that traffic is lowest in the
--month -of August. and maximum in January due to more local traffic

with Sunday and Monday showing lower traffic than other days. witi

addition of shoulders, this can be increased by a maximum of 20% to
about 48,000 PCU/day.
The heavy traffic on the existing road have led to congestion, delays
and accidents. Congestion results in excessive fuel consumption and
increased air pollution due to auto exhaust emissions.
The 'No Project' (No Build) scenario may only preivent the marginal
rise in the ambient air quality and noise levels during the construction
phase of the proposed highway which may not be -very significant.
- -   -  -     ~    However, in the operation phase, the result of increased traffic through
the village will lead to increased population exposure to air and noise
pollution. It also will result in:
-  -  ,                          .    increased travel time;
I - . increased vehicle operating costs; and
increased accidents.
The 'No Project' scenario would in fact be a major setback to an
effective solution to traffic hazards and associated problems. It would
arrest  the  possible -significant  enhancement  and  economic
development of the entire region.

highway and provides better level of service like high design speeds for
-similar traffic volumes. The project would help reduction of the traffic

A perusal of the data presented in the above table clearly reveals a
steady rise of traffic densities on the existing road in the limiting and
normal capacities with No Project Scenario. In the limiting capacity
scenario, the maximum rise in VIC ratio is likely to be at 3.36 against the
present ratio of 0.55 with No Project Scenario. Similarly, the ratio for
With Project Scenario will be 0.82 in the year 2030 with respect to the
existing ratio of 0.13.
Whereas with the normal capacity, the rise in volume/capacity ratio is
likely to be at 6.79 against the present ratio of 1.11 with No Project
Scenario. Similarly, the ratio for With Project Scenario will be 1.27 in the
year 2030 with respect to the existing ratio of 0.21.

the project implementation will bypass the main villages along

6.0        ENVIRONMENT MANAGEMENT PLAN'
[                   6.1        INTRODUCTION
An  Environmental Management  Plan  (EMP)  has  been
recommended in this -chapter which highlights all concemed
environmental issues, mitigatory actions to be taken and the
agencies responsible for implementation of these measures.
The  present project will however have  some  negative
environmental impact-s on the ambient air quality and noise levels   -- 
during the construction phases. Other issues such as green belt
removal and land acquisition are also related to the project.
Although some of these impacts. especially air and noise impacts
cannot be fully avoided. measures can be taken towards
reduction of these impacts by way of mitigatory measures. The
project has overall positive impacts by way of reduction in travel
time, congestion and accidents. Also, in terms of prominent
-environmental impacts of road projects on air quality and noise
levels, the project brings considerable improvement to possible
zexposure levels to population when compared with no project
scenario.
6.2        IMPACT DURING CONSTRUCTION PHASE
6.2.1      Impact on Air Quality
During construction period the impacts on air quality are mainly
due to the material movement and the actual construction         !  -
F,                            activities.
Due to material movement air quality over a large area is affected
though, not in significant levels. There is an increase in the dust
levels all along the haul roads, the borrow areas and the dumping
areas.                                       -
L,:                           The emissions from the construction machinery are the major
source of -ambient air-pollution;during the actual construction.
* Continuous use of construction machinery and generator sets
may cause rise in -ambient air pollution levels. The degree of
impact may be higher during winter season and during night time.

Mitigation Measures
Mitigation Measures for Mobile Source Emissions
In prder to curb the increased fugitive dust emissions in the
area due to vehicular movement and raw  material
'transport, provisions should be made for sprinkling of
water on all the haul roads in the area. Sprinkling of water
should be carried out at least twice a day on a regular
basis during the entire construction period especially in the
winter and summer seasons. Special attention should be
given to all the haul roads passing through residential
areas in the region. Daily inspection at hali roads and at
construction site should be carried out to ensure removal
of construction debris to the landfill sites;
Dust covers should be used over the beds of trucks which
will be used for the transportation of materials prone to
fugitive dust emissions. Additionally any of these materials
which may collect on the horizontal surfaces of these
trucks  during - loading  should  be  removed  before
transportation;
Construction requiring street closings in heavy traffic areas
should be performed during off-peak hours;
Idling of delivery trucks or other equipments should not be
permitted during periods when they are being unloaded or
.. { -            ...                     . are not in active use;
- ..;.*  Concrete should be supplied from an on site batching plant
in order to reduce travel distances of concrete delivery
trucks; and
---*  -Low emission (diesel) construction vehicles and generator
-     f; -                      .             sets should be used wherever possible.
-Mitigation Measures for Fixed Source Emissions
* -DLmNOx emission from the construction yards iis expected to
have a some  influence- up to 1 km distance on the
downwind side under adverse meteorologic,al conditions.
Hence, it is desirable that construction yard should not be
located within 1 km of major villages like Jarod, Asoj.
: ~- ;+.i                                    Khandewadi and Baska;
; i - Within the construction yard the stationary equipments
should be located as far as practicable from  receptor
locations in order to allow dispersion of emitted pollutants;

6.2.2       Impact on Noise Levels
f       -    -                 The pri.fne sources of noise levels during the construction phase
are the construction machinery and the vehicular noise due to
material movement at the site. Though the effect of noise would
be insignificant during daytime, -the residential areas located in
L.                             the near vicinity of the construction site may experience increase
in the night time ambient noise levels. (Refer Action Sheet - 3,
Table-6.5).
Mitigation Measures
Li                            .      Construction  contracts should  specify   that   the
construction equipments should meet the noise and air
'~j /  -            -emission levels as pe'r-EPA Rules, 1986.
The siting of construction yards sites should be done
leaving at least 100 m distance from any residential areas
in the project area except at major villages like Jarod, Asoj,
Khandewadi and Baska where it desirable that it should be      F:
i                           - - -    1 'km; away due-to air pollution control requirements and
specifi6ally at least 250 m away from the school at Jarod
which will allow noise to attenuate; and
L                         - . -      The main stationary noise producing sources such as the
generator sets'etc. ~should be-provided with noise shields
around them.''The noise shields can either be a brick
L                   -    -   -  -  masonry structure '-or-any other physical barrier which is
effective in adequate'attenuation of noise levels. A three
meter high enclosure made up of brick and mud with
- ^ - --      - internal plastering-:of a non-reflecting surface will be very
effective in these regards. For protection of construction
workers, ear plugs should be,provided to those working
-very close to the noise generating machinery.

6.2.3      Impact on Water Quality
The proposed project will not alter the existing water quality on a
permanent basis, but during the construction phase extent of
surface runoff and silt load may increase giving rise to a negative
impact on receiving natural water bodies.
Mitigation Measures
Runoffs from the construction site should be passed
through silt traps.
6.2.4      Sanitation at Workers Colony
Sewage.. and the domestic solid waste generated at the
construction workers colony would have a negative impact on the
aesthetics and environment of the surrounding area, if not
disposed off in.an efficient manner.
Mitigation Measures
The worker's camp must not be located within 1 km of the
villages Jarod, Asoj, Khandewadi and Baska; and
The construction.workers should be allotted a specified
area of land on which the workers temporary colony
should be built. Adequate sanitary facilities, drainage,
washing and toilet facilities with septic tanks and refuse
collection and disposal should be provided to the workers.
The provision. of water supply and toilet facililies should be
made as per the stipulated guidelines in Indian Labour Act.
'..   '.Refer Action Sheet - 4, Tables-6.6

6.3        IMPACT DURING OPERATIONAL PHASE
- The. operation -of the proposed highway would be socio-
-    -, o-;    -  - 0   -~~economically beneficial to the entire area due to a reliable and
fast transport- facility with a high level of service. Traffic
congestion and  resulting delays and acciidents would be
minimized on all other roads of the area.
A rise in air pollution in the areas  adjacent to and in the
ii J -- =downwind direction of the road will be observed as the traffic
grows in future. The observed levels will be however lower than
the expected levels as smooth traffic flow will reduce the emission
levels per unit distance traveled. Also noise levels will be lower
due to re-routing of the alignment away from the population
centers.
6.3.1      Impact on Air Quality
After the project implementation the ambient air quality levels in
future years will be better with respect to without project scenario.
However, as the traffic increases, the ambient iair quality levels
will also increase marginally. The effect will be more pronounced
during winter season when -night time ground level inversion are
observed in the area.
Mitigation Measures
-  ~*    The most effective -control methods of air pollution due to
vehicular emissions is to use fuel efficient engines,
introduction of catalytic converters for petrol vehicles and
use of smoke traps for diesel vehicles;
~-     ;i*    It should be made compulsory for all vehicles to adhere to
the engine maintenance schedules and CPCB standards
-o reduce -air pollution due to vehicular emissions.
Considering the fact that buses from a low percentage
,         .         ;  z      3 3 - ^ ;~   (6%) of existing traffic, there is a scope to improve public
ItrAnsport which may reduce the two wheelers traffic and
thereby the air emissions;
-     Along the highway plantation of trees such as neem which
- has known properties to absorb HC is recommended. Care
- should however be taken to space the trees so as not to
restrict movement of air; and
- --. ,Development of landscape along the main road can bring
about 30 percent reduction of concentration of pollutants
at the ground level. It is therefore recommended that the
area available on both sides of the highway should be
used to develop a green belt  with dense canopy to

The main purpose of these exercises is to know the peoples
reaction to the perceived impact of proposed road on the people
at individual and settlement level. Visits were made to know the
villages and key persons in the villages. The meeting, with key
residents and opinion leaders of the villages and llocal NGOs who
are prominent, good, genuine and committed to the task assigned
;X were.organized-through-the social survey teamr  to explain the
purpose and scope of study during the period March 25-26, 1997.
The, views of the villagers were elicited  after distribution  of
Information Brochure / Leaflet. Formal meetings were organized
at major villages along the project route. These proceedings
emphasized on the community profile  of the most affected
-villages including their problems, basic issues and concern over
acquisition of pond, land and temple etc. The details of the public
.--   informnation and consultation are summarised in Annexure II.

6.6         RISK ASSESSMENT
If the Environmenit Management Plan is implemented completely,
there will not any environmental risk in the implementation of the
project. The proposed alignment bypasses the villages Jarod,
Asoj, Khandewadi and Baska as also the other villages thereby
limiting the adjerse impacts of air and noise pollution within
L                              acceptable limits; This also reduces the social risk as there are
minimal R&R a.zpects to be tackled. This is an existing highway
where the cross drainage works provided have been found to be
effective (inspite of blockages in the drainage no flooding have
been recorded). As the proposed project -will be strengthening the
; .    .       ---    . : . .                              -Kirloskar Consultants Umited
r



Environmental Impact Assessmel                           Chapter 6
Vadodara - Halol Road                    EnvironmentManagement Plan
existing CD works and proposing new ones adjacent to the
existing ones, hence the CD works is not an issue of significance
in this project.
However, as the proposed project road will lead to continued and
increased transportation of hazardous substanc:es, even if they
are expected to follow the Environmental Regulations regarding
storage, handling and transport, it is necessary to provide for
some back-up facilities in the event of an accident.  It is
recommended that the following facilities be provided as a part of
the project:
- i. ;ambulance stationed at the toll booth
ii.  'tow truck-for removing damaged vehicles
ii-.  -communication facilities at each (and in between) ends
iv.   access to fire-local (district) fighting facilities.
In the current operation scenario of the project, it is necessary for
the project operator to undertake additional responsibility for
above backup facilities in view of the stakes involved in operation
of the toll road. However, like any other nationaVstate highway,
the operator's responsibility is nevertheless limitedl since it is the
district administration's job to highway accidents.

\ j   :  0-  Vadodara - Halol Road                         Resettlement Action Plan    i
;   ',, 1.1  :   .Objectives Of Social Assessment Study
Road improvement includes realignment of road at certain chainages
which may involve expropriation of land from the current owners/users.
Expropriation of land, eviction of squatters and removal of agricultural
-encroachments from  Right Of Way (ROW) gives rise to social
L -                        disruption and economic loss to the persons and families who derive
economic benefits from those -assets.
According to detailed pre-feasibility study carried out by IL&FS, the
existing ROW of state highway between Vadodara and Halol is 24 m
!--  :   :                except at the curve -points where it extends upto 30 m. The proposed
L, ?-:    ':   '           fwidening and road improvements require 60 m wide strip of land at the
straight sections and upto 70 m wide strip at the curves. This additional
area betw6en the existing ROW and land required for the widened (i.e.
a strip of 36 m all along sections running along the existing road) and
improved road is defined as "Corridor of Impact" and forms the basis
for identification of Project Affected Persons (PAPs).
The main objective of the social assessment exercise is to assess the
social impact of the project, identify issues and assess consequent risk
to the project due to acquisition of land and assets as well as negative
social impacts, outline  Resettlement and  Rehabilitation  (R&R)
measures to mitigate.the negative-impacts and risks due to the project.
The remedial measures suggested in the mitigation plan must ensure
that all the> PAPs have their income levels after the project
rF                        implementation improved or at least :restored to the pre-project levels.
This leads to visualise the whole exercise as composed of three main
components viz (i) a General Social Impact Assessment, (ii) Public
Information and -Consultation, the results of which will become the
-.base for (iii) a Resettlement Action 'Plan (RAP).
r      -        -  1.2   Study Process
The process. of social assessment involves primarily the following
steps:
L                         1.   General Assessment    This process  involves enlisting
chainagewise -all Ihe PAPs' 'iPublic and  Privately owned
;  i- ;    (movable and fixed) structures, Trees (fruit bearing and non-fruit
- E ;S  . bearing) -belonging to the land owners etc. and their exact
distances -from the- central line of the existing ROW. The
V                -                f . d outcome- of this exercise' gives the idea about the chainagewise
amount of the losses involved in the.project implementation and
.. . - serves"as- the first order'approximation to the final risk
I   -              '   .~~~~~~calculatingexercise-.     , ,5,,;;; ;. 
2.   Identification and Profiling of PAPs * This process is followed
by general assessment wherein Head of every Project Affected'
L                               Family (PAF) is,,interviewed with respect to his socio-economic
aspects thoroughly and, the-.responses--are--recorded in a
Anx. -  -1      Kirloskar Consultants Limited
Anx. I. -1 
I;     ' i   0' ~                          -  :0t,.',': 



4-; 
Environmental Impact Assessment                                   Annexure
Vadodara - Halof Road                                  Resettlement Action Pt7'
detailed questionnaire especially designed for this survey. Thir
exercise generates a very important and useful database for tl-
further analysis and decision making.
3.    Development of Draft RAP  After analyzing the informatior
-collected in the identification and profiling of PAP exercise, dra.
version of Resettlement Action Plan is developed.
4.    Public Information and Consultations (PIC): Keeping in vie-
the draft version of RAP. an -exercise is initiated (1) tu
understand in depth the sentiments and tihe feelings of the
iJ         , ,,,,   ,  ,, ,        >  - -pedple losing their assets in the project ancl (2) to identify the
social and environmental issues/problems ait large so that nL'
such issue remains unaddressed as well as to take care of the
problems as perceived by the people losing their assets due t(
project implementations.
5.    Finalization of RAP  The RAP is finalized Ibased on the draf.
version of the RAP and findings of the PIC exercise.
6.   Identification of Institutional Arrangement: As the final step
of SA, institutionat arrangements for efficient implementation of
RAP and monitoring of its outcome are suggested.
----,,--'-:---        1.3         Progression ofthe Study
The social assessment study was initiated in August 1996 and the
focus was on the general assessment, identification and profiling of the
> - -5,: .";'! ~ '' , ' structure  PAPs.--, ..... ,.-----
A set of PIC was conducted in March 1997 to assess the project
.,awareness among the villagers of the project area, to inform them
about the project design, to understand their general views, opinions,
sentiments pertaining to the project, to identify major issues and issues
in commonality and obtain consent on draft R&R measures. The major
outcomes of PIC exercise are briefed in Annexure II.
1.4         Minimisation Of Adverse Social Impact
The initial verification exercise carried out by the consultants identified
594 structures in 31.5 Kms road length. Since 395 structures (66.5 %)
of the total, structures. (594) are coming in only thiree villages viz.,
Jarod, Asoj and Baska, it has been proposed to bypass these villages
in mutual consultation with local population in these villages.
- The resettlement issues at another enroute village Khandewadi were
minimized by reducing the proposed ROW  of 60 m to 36 m and
acquiring the land on the east of the existing carriageway in order to
avoid the demolition of a settlement of huts (44).

[               1.6          Legal Procedure for LA
For.any LA, the Act under section (u/s) - 4(1) stipulates publication of a
notification to that effect in -the official Gazette and in two daily
L~ 0          R       7     newspapers circulated in the locality of which at least one shall be in
the regional language. Such a notification enables the concemed
authorities to enter upon the land, make preliminary survey, ascertain
L                   <       its suitability, and determine,the exact portion of the land to be
acquired. The substance -of this notification is given as public notice at
a convenient place in the locality in which the land to be acquired is
located. Such a notification provides an opportunity to the parties
interested in the land to file-objections if any, u/s-5A to the proposed
acquisition.
'in order to proceed, with the.acquisition of the notified land or any
portion of it, a declaration u/s-6 of the Act specifies the precise
L,                          boundaries of -the area of the land to be taken and has to be issued
within one year from the date of notification u/s - 4. Every declaration is
published in the official Gazette and in two daily newspapers with
circulation in the local area. With the appearance of the notification uls-
6 .of the Act ..in the,(Gazette, Govemment in Revenue Department
issues.direction to -the ,Collector u/s - 7 of the Act to take order for the
|, ,                         E acquisition --,of ootified -Jland -and/or --other immovable properties.,  . .
-Thereafter,, Section-8 -of Ahe-Act requires land ..to be marked out,   f -.
measured and. planned,---while - u/s-9 notices are served to the
concemed persons'stating the intention of the Govemment to take
possession of the land and that any claims for compensation should be.
made to the Collector.-Finally, the award of compensation is made by
-the Collector u/s-11 of the Act after inquiring into objections (if any) in
,, .                       ,; : . . . . E a, : pursuant to the notice given uls-9, to'-the measurements made u/s-8
and into the -value:-of ihe- lad - on' the date of publication of the
notification u/s 4(1). The award u/s - 11- is made within two years from
-.the date of publication of the declaration u/s - 6 and if no award is
made within this period, the entire proceedings of the LA is lapsed and
fresh notification u/s_4(1) - needs to, be initiated. Once the award is
passed, the Collector-takes possession of the land. which there upon
vests absolutely in the Gove'rnment free from - all encumbrances..      -'
However, in case of urgency Section-17 of the Act empowers the
Collector to take poss.ession -of,-the--land even though 'no award has
