1.7-        R&R Policy
R&R policy for the project has been formulated in accordance with the 
World Bank Guidelines on Involuntary Resettlemenit (OD 4.30) and'
Environment and Social Report ( a policy statement on environmental
.   and social issues) of the IL&FS. The VHRP lpolicy takes into
consideration those coming within the 'corridor of ne.gative impact' of'
the project: Essentially they are in two broad categoriies.
*  those with private ownership of land and other immovable
assets; and
-encroachers on government land and squatters within the
corridor of impa'ct.
The main objective of deriving, the entitlement through compensation
and assistance to-be given to the PAPs during R&R is to ensure that
.*.   heir standards of living is. at least restored to the pre-project level and
preferably improved above the levels they enjoyed before the
implementation of ihe project. In the absence of any legal regulatory
framework to govem such entitlements associated with resettlement
and rehabilitation projects, IL&FS has adopted its own standards for
entitlement framework as furnished below in Table - 2.

1.8        Coverage Of Socio-Economic Profile Survey Of PAPs
The exercise of conducting  a socio-economic survey of the PAPs
generated socio-economic profiles of PAPs who were categorized in to
three main groups, viz., (1) Land holders, (2) Tenants working on
agricultural lands and  (3) PAPs loosing  residentiaUcommercial
structures but not fatling under categories (1) or (2) above to ascertain *;
their pre-project socio-economic status so as:
-  to ensure that the entitlement for each PAP is in line with the R&R I
policy; and
to provide the baseline economical status for post-project
monitoring of the implementation of the R&R measures.
Due to the difficulties experienced in identifying the land owners, as the
land records were not updated, the survey was unidertaken for only
those 50 PAFs (owning 66 plots) who could be corresctly identified as
the land owners. This needs to be followed up by a separate survey of
each PAF when the correct status of the land ownership is established.
In the case of tenants working on agricultural lands, these people
manage the lands on a seasonal (or longer) basis. The period of the
observation was in non- cropping season and therefore the tenants
were not available at the agricultural fields. As their place of residence
'and  location of agricultural lands is often different and they were
engaged in other occupation during the non-cropping period, the
tenants could not be contacted. However, extensive inquiries with the
-i    -     . - :villagers helped to generate partial list of the tenants. Accordingly, 56
land tenants were identified cultivating on 68 plots in six villages.
In'case of 10 PAFs ioosing residential structures, their socio-economic
-profiling has been done.,
A complete profiling of all the remaining commercial and public
-structures has been done which largely falls into categorizes:
* movable shops
damage to fencing and compound wall
community structures.

Land Owner PAFs

Land Tenants
In the absence of information about the profile of land tenants, the
entitlemeht for tenants is based on the estimated land under tenancy
from the plot numbers identified. The total land under tenancy is,
estimated as 25.45 ha (16.72 ha. - irrigated and 7.73 ha.- unirrigated).
The patter of land plot sizes is assumed to be similar to the land-
holdings under LA from private owners. Since only severely affected
tenants are to be provided land equal to MEL, it is assumed that such
requirements will be limited to 10 tenants and the remaining will be
assisted through loans / grants.
Industrial Land
Only one'case of industrial land loss of a defunct factory exists. The
compensation will be based on the land value estimated through
inquiries with Gujarat Industrial Development Boarcd (being industrial
land).
Loss of Village Land
The public land acquired within the revenue boundary of the village can
belong to either the Gram Panchayat or the Revenue Department,
GoG. In case of the govemment land, since the GoG is a member
(shareholder) of SPV, arnd since the project (road) will be transferred
back to the govemment, no compensation is proposed to be paid for
-   :                       ~       such govemment lands. As the gram panchayat / government land
holding is yet to be identified through JMS, it is proposed to pay a price
to the Gram Panchayat for such lands at the government rate from the
contingency provision.
1.11.2     Loss of Structures
Residential Houses
Ten houses are to be demolished for the proposed rcad improvement.
Seven of these houses are occupied by the owneirs themselves, 2
houses -have been rented to tenants and 1 house! is owned by a
squatter.
As per IL&FS policies, all house owners are compensated by a house
of area equal to the area of the lost structure. In case the area of the
house is less than the minimum govemment norm for single family
dwelling, a'house of -minimum area is provided (Indira Awas Vikas
Yojana). For squatters, irrespective of the size for the present dwelling,
an ownership house of minimum area as per govermment norm is
provided. The beneficiary under this case however cannot sell the
house till completion of the concession period of the project.
In the case -of VHRP all residential structures are below 32.5 sq.m.
(350 sq.ft.) in.area and therefore all entitlement are restricted to houses
with built-up';area of 32.5 sq.m. and surrounding open area of equal
dimension.       -.
In the case of tenants, it is expected that they will re-establish
themselves elsewhere and the lL8FS policy entitles them only for a
. ........                    shifting allowance. However in the present cases, the tenants belong to

1.12        Institutional Arrangement For Implementing The RAP
1.12.1      implementation Responsibility
It is primarily the responsibility of the Special Purpose Vehicle (SPV) to
ensure successful implementation of the project. The construction of
L         - '7f X -   <  the project mainly involves acquisition of private agricultural land to the
extent of  more than 85% of the total requirement. The entitlement
fi   e:; . - -.  - . . -: C    package developed -for individual PAFs indicate that about 20%
families will require -a grant/loan/alternate land for restoration of their  L;  :
pre-project  status.  Adequate  institutional  arrangements  for
operationalizing the 'mechanism  for assistance is necessary for
-successful-implementation of RAP. Therefore there is a need to focus
on two main issues:
*  Issues related to LA and -alternate land allocation for severely
affected PAPs; and ,.-
*  Issues related to Economic Rehabilitation (ER) of PAFs.
Land acquisition (LA) is primarily the government responsibility and a
mutually acceptable compensation at market rate is to be arrived at
-   through negotiations. The SPV is committed to pay the difference
between the compensation amount as per govemment rates and the
negotiated settlemenf price of the land. The procedure therefore will
L  ? . ...   -   ->-   e -   -                               ;    Kirloskar Consultants Umited     -
Anx. I-11-



-Environmental Impact Assessment                         -  AnneuXre I
Vadodara - Halol Road                             Resettlement Action Plan _
involve interaction and consensus building with the PAFs on an
agreeable fair rate of compensation.
Economic Rehabilitation of PAFs is also necessary so that their pre-
project income levels are enhanced-or at least retained. It is essential
that the compensation paid to the PAFs is utilized to build assets for i
substituting'the loss of income to the PAFs. Since grants and loans are
proposed to be given to the severely affected PAFs as means to raise
the income levels, in addition to compensation for land (cash or land;
equal to MEL), it is necessary to identify.local business activities or
provide vocational training to utilize these grants/loans to build assets.
It is proposed to set up a committee as under:
Project Level R&R Implementation and Monitoring Committee
to ensure that the proposed ER measures including awards of
compensations are being implemented as planned, make policy
decisions to facilitate implementation of the same and give
necessary administrative decisions/directions as required.
In this context, it is necessary that a NGO experienced in LA
procedures and in implementing income generation schemes work
closely  with SPV  and- govemment. Thus the implementation
responsibility through an institutional arrangement will need to be
developed  around  the  SPV, GoG  Representative  and  NGO
involvement.
1.12.2    liWlementation Schedule
The implementation schedule depends largely on the decision of
contracting the project in two or more stretches. The schedule
proposed below assumes that i will be taken up in two stretches
simultaneously. Land should be taken into possession and handed
over to project authorities (preferably free from all encumbrances) for
carrying out constructional activities.
The first activity of the project, as far as LA is concemred is the issue of
.a notification u/s -4(1) of the LA Act, 1894 as amended in 1984. LA
takes minimum of 9-months to complete and it was initiated in October
1996. However, its further follow-up as described in clhapter 9 is yet to
-  commence. Compensation needs lo be paid to the IPAPs before the
- start -of actual project construction. In case of any delay in LA
:proceedings, R&B will take lands from their owners on 'consent basis'.
The implementation schedule is reflected in the annual phasing of
budget funds wherein it is assumed that 75% land acquisition and
-'related ER will be done in the first year itself, while the balance will be
done in the second year. Implementation schedule of 2 years has been
worked out on-the general-LA proceedings. Delays if any in the LA
cannot be specifically addressed. However, to accelerate the process
of acquiring land, negotiated settlement can be given top priority.
The activities.to be taken on an immediate basis as al part of initiating
the implementation of the RAP are as follows:

1.0        Public Information And Consultations
f i   0        1.1         Introduction
Public Information and Consultations (PIC) exercise was conducted
with following aim:
t*  o understand in depth the sentiments and feelings of the people
losing their assets in the project;
-_     -              . . ~to identify the social and environmental issues/problem at large so
that no such issue remains unaddressed; and
L                          - to resolve satisfactorily problems as perceived by the people losing
their assets due to project implementation.
1.2        PIC Process
rn'                        Since the proposed project is linear in nature and very few structures
I.;                        come inAthe proposed ROW except for.the villages Jarod, Asoj and
Baska, it was decided to conduct PIC in
* two villages which are densely populated viz. Bhaniyara and Halol;
- three villages Jarod, Asoj & Baska, where bypasses are proposed:
l--                           and
* Khandewadi village located adjacent to the existing highway and a
unique feature of loss of pond area was to occur due to the
rproposed alignment.
In each of .these villages a group of farmers, land owners.
businessmen,  agricultural  labourers,  village  Tahsildar,  Gram
L             -              - . . . \ - 0 Panchayat- officials, some of the PAPs of the project, women and
teachers were selected to cover socio-economic cross-section of the
village. The group was also selected well in advance and the time
schedule of PIC-was informed to them three days prior to conducting
PIC in the respective village. A pamphlet in Gujarati language (local
language) -was carefully prepared detailing major aspects and      .:
concems -of this project from social and environmental view. The
pamphlets were distributed:to the group.members ten minutes before
the PIC. -
Each PIC was initiated by detailing of the project needs and its
justification by giving village specific observations made throughout this
l        ;         .- >   study to the group. These -observations were mainly pertaining to traffic
projections, reduced -safety and environmental aspects in the absence
and presence of the project, environmental resources such as pond
etc. This was followed .by the description about the social &
environmental  -.mitigation 'process  planned  in  the   project
implementation. Also the larger economic prospects were highlighted
L in these intersections. This established an important linkage between
the group and the -consultants through an informal and friendly
r  f--   -                                      -
Kirloskar Consultants Limited

;L 



* .EnvironmentalImpact Assessment                              Annexure i
Vadodara - Halol Road                     Public Information and Consultations_.
atmosphere creation which allowed the group members to freely' 
discuss their concems.
All the members of the group were invited to express themselves in
various capacities. The opinions of the group members were extremely-,
z' ;.'-~.'   '                   useful for the development-of a consensus for the! alignment as a.
whole after classifying and analysing each and every group member's'
opinion. Various altematives for mitigation measures on general
environmental & social issues were also worked out.
Also on an individual basis wherever possible alignment of road was_
given a thought for all possible modifications.
A number of PAP-specific mitigation measures were evolved,_
discussed, evaluated and documented on the spot. Major social and..'
land acquisition related issues were discussed at an individual basis
and perceived need based solutions were suggested.
Thus the PlC process resulted in a general understanding of the'
project needs and constraints and gave confidence to the group that
they were important partLiers in the development process. A win-win'
situation wherever feasible was evolved which is intended to serve as
a guideline while developing the RAP.

the users residing in the on-route villages or those who have
land in these villages but stay in Vadodara.
S    -  .e. *A follow-up meeting was requested to discuss the above issues
During the PIC, the villagers -were told that a humane and just
entitlement package,  including  rehabilitation  measures  where
I .               necessary, will be worked out for each project affected person (PAP)
through a participatory process with adequate representation of PAPs
and community representatives. Villagers were told that the project as
such did--not have much  scope for direct employment. The
rehabilitation  measures, however, will be  developed  through
professional advise and will be compatible with the skills and market
conditions in the project area. One road crossing at the village is
considered essential and the consultants have been asked to propose
a cost effective solution.:,

-1.4       -Modifications in RAP
Any project must be based on the rational choice of the people
especially those who might be adversely affected by it. Only such at
approach ensure both that displacement is minimised and the projec
really serves the public purpose. Peoples' participation in formulatinm
and selection of a project also ensures that those who are going to bE
-PAPs are not made victims but rather partners in the development.
The PIC conducted through participatory approach has led to followinc
major modifications in the RAP.
* Free toll for project land owners: All the PAPs whose land is
acquired should be permitted to free use of highway. This may be
regulated by allowing only one commerciail and one non-
commercial vehicle per affected family. After due verification of the
PAPs, the project can issue a letter to PAP to use highway without
paying toll.
-  Private lands and houses which gets isolated or cut off due to
-  :  - -!                         acquisition of land in the project, may also be acquired if the owner
gives his/her consentto such acquisition. This would apply only to
oustees who can not make economic or any other use of their non-
acquired assets, e.g. Bypass at Baska divides the defunct factory
(Furfural Chemicals Pvt. Ltd.) premises into two disjoint parts of
which the-company can not make economic use. And bypass at
Asoj village generates a triangular section of land owned by Mr.
Ashwin Sheth and his family members between existing road and
. .      . .--the proposed bypass. This triangular section ol land can not be
utilised further for economic purpose by the owner and hence
should be purchased by the project.
*  Changes in the curve Alignment: During PIIC, all altemative
leading to non-displacement were studied. Different altematives on
the basis of technical and social merits and demerits of the project
were examined and made available to public. It has led to many
t .  i:. . t    .   - .--<-   - - ,- -..changes in RAP. Firstly, land at the curve aligniment in the Asoj
bypass belonging to Mr. Ashwin Sheth has been improved in order
to minimise the loss in the utility of his land after land acquisition. At
..many places, minor curve -improvement or reduction in ROW
subject to technical feasibility and social acceptability are made.
*7 6.                               Secondly, in three project villages, need for access to the other side
of the road was strongly demanded by the villagers which led to
provision  -of underpasses in the final design. This has also
enhanced safety levels of the villagers and animals crossing the
road.
.  ..-..,   . .  . 
1.5     D Concluding Remarks
The PIC process has resulted in acceptance of the proposed
alignment, identification of community concem at large and possible
Kirlosiar C onsultants limited
Anx. 11 -12



Environmental Impact Assessment                                Annexure I      E
Vadodara - HaloJ Road                      Public Information and Consultations  [
acceptable solutions, both at individual level and community level.
L                            These will be generalised approximately while formulating the RAP.
It needs to be mentioned here that discussions with each PAP should
be done as a follow-up by the NGO, exploring the available solutions
suiting the individual considering his/her socio-economic background
so as to ensure adherence to the IL & FS R&R policy.

4.0        Upper WtndlUpper Air'Climatological Data
The upper. air climatological data at the nearest station to Vadodarn
recording uipper w(inds, is Ahmedabad situated at a idistance of 80 km it
the North of Vadodara. This data is presented be!low for ground anc
elevated inversion layers at 0530 (0000 GMT) and '1730 (1200 GMT) ir
Tables A3.1 - 14 and A3.1 - 15. The early morningi mixing heights are
given inTable A3.1 - 16.
Inversions, and Stable Layers Near Ground:
A stable layer could either form at the ground or at an elevation. In the
latter case, it becomes necessary to completely defirne it by its base, top
- and thickness. The base has been taken as the level at which the lapse
rate leading to stability commences, the top, where it terminates with the
difference between the two -providing the thickness. For the study ol
-frequency distribution of, stable layers, the atmcisphere within the
.boundary layer (1.5 kin) has been divided in to thirte-en layers, first ten
are at an interval of 100 m followed by two layers of 250 m each by the
Indian Meteorological Department.
Data available on stable layers (ground based and ellevated inversions).
*     ~~both for morning (00 -GMT) and afternoon (12 GMTr) data have been
compiled from.the-.data published ~by India Meteorological Department.
'Percentage frequencies of stable layers (Inversioni) both for ground
--based and elevated inversions, in respect of Ahamedabad are tabulated
~in Tables-A3.1 -14 and A3.1- 15.

GMT hours for Ahmedabad is given in Table-3.1 - 14.

5.0         Mixing Heights for Ahmedabad
L                             The urban moming mixing heights calculated for selected Indian urban
centres by Mr. B.Satyanarayana, Department of Meteorology and
Oceanography, Andhra University are complied for AHMEDABAD. The
L                             heat island magnitudes computed by the Summers formula are used as
the temperature correction factors for estimating the urban morning
mixing heights, since the actual urban rural temperature differences
were not available. The estimated monthly morning mixing heights for
Ahmedabad are given in Table-A3.1 - 16.

Environmental Impact Assessment                              Appendix 4.1
Vadodara - Halol Road                           Air Quality Simulation Results
APPENDIX 4.1
AIR QUALITY SIMULATION RESULTS
While modelling computations, two typical wind cases viz., paraallel and
cross wind cases have been considered for developing wind case
scenarios. The parallel wind case assumes wind direction to be parallel
(2700) to the general road layout. The cross wind case assumes the wind
direction to be crossing (1800) the general road layout. The predicting
distances are measured from the edge of ROW (Right Of Way) which is
reportedly 24 m and 60 m for 'Do Nothing' and 'With Project' scenarios
respectively. Since the respective receptor locations (distances) changes
with respect to the project scenafios, the concentrations predicted at
these receptor locations are not comparable with each other.
The wind velocity of 1 m/s and mixing height of 90 m were considered
for modelling as a worst case.
Simulation for CO
The peak hourly (1800 hour) CO for 2000 A.D. and 2015 A.D. was
predicted for all stability classes and are presented in Figures-A4.1-1,
A4.1-3, A4.1-5, and A4.1-7 for 'With Project Scenario'. And same has
been depicted in Figures-A4.1-2, A4.1-4, A4.1-6 and A4.1-8 for 'Do
Nothing Scenario'. Two cases for wind directions, parallel wind
(Figures-4.1, 4.2, 4.5 and 4.6) and cross wind (Figures-4.3. 4.4, 4.7
and 4.8), respectively, were considered.
For the case of eight hourly (from 1600 hour to 2300 hour) CO,
predictions were done by assigning hourly stability class for both the
wind cases.
2.         Peak Hourly Simulation for NOx-
In this case also, both parallel and cross wind cases were modelled for E
and F stability classes respectively in the background of maximum
concentration being predicted only for these combination of wind cases
and stability classes than any other such combinations.
Kirloskar Consultants Limited
A4.1-1

Environmental Impact Assessment                               Appendix 6.1
Vadodara - Halol Road                                  Landscaping Scheme
The landscaping has been suggested at the following four locations.
Figure-A6-1 depicts the scheme on the cross-section of the road.
Surface road side plantation
This includes mixed plantation of large, shady flowering and non-
flowering trees on outer side of both the service roads along the entire
road at 6 m c/c (centre to centre). With additional trees of 10% over the
estmated trees, this will result in approximately 350 trees for a length of
1 km inclusive of outer side of both service roads with considered. The
unit cost per tree has been estimated by considering the following
aspects:
a)         Excavating the pit of size 1.00 m x 1.00 m x 1.00 m deep
@ Rs.10/-;
b)         Filling of the excavated pit by farm yard manure and
garden soil in 1:2 proportion @ Rs. 120/-;
c)         Sapling as per plan @ Rs. 25/-;
d)         Plantation of sapling including provision of side supports,
watering etc., @ Rs. 25/-; and
e)         Labour charges @ Rs. 10/- .
The approximate total cost involved for 350 trees per km at the total unit
cost of Rs. 200/- per tree and with additional cost of tree guard of barbed
wire fencing @ Rs. 2,00,000/- per km comes out to be Rs. 400,000/-.
Highway side tree plantation
With smaller but moderate trees at lesser c/c distance of 5 m, it is
assumed that road cross-section will have 6 rows (3 on either side of the
carriageway) .This will give about 1600 trees/km. The cost of this
plantation at Rs. 200/- is estimated to be Rs. 32,000/- per km.
* Plantation of shrubs on slopes
This could be in the form of Bouganvellia or flowerbed or grass cover
at the rate of Rs. 60 per sq. m. For an area of 6 sq. m between two trees
of 3 m apart and with such 100 patches on both sides of the road, a
lumpsum provision of Rs. 72000/- has been made for the planatation
along the road slope.
Plantation in central median
Thick median plantation of Casurina trees at Im c/c in 4 rows is
assumed. This will result in 4000 saplings/km. The unit cost of Rs. 25/-
per tree has been estimated by cxonsidering the lesser pit size of 500 m
x 500 m x 500 m including excavation, filling of the pit with garden soil
Kirloskar Consultants Limited

EnWronmentaiand Socal Assessment Repoft                          Chapter-7
Vadodara - Hakal Road                          Social Assesment -An hiNrodueflon
Social assessment study for the project, therefore, aims at identifying
the PAPs, analyzing their socio-economic status, assessing losses
due to project implementation and propose Resettlement and
Rehabilitation plan which ensures that PAPs do not suffer economic
losses. Thus, the Detailed Feasibility Study for the project, besides
examining the technical, economical and financial feasibility of the
proposed project, aims at carrying out an environmental and social
impact assessment of the proposed project to identify the risks
involved and the mitigation measures thereof. The environmental and
social assessment study is expected to prepare a well researched and
documented environmental and social assessment report (ESAR) with
*                         due consideration to public opinion and sensitivities. The ESAR is to
be prepared according to the principles and processes contained in
the Environmental -and Social Statement of IL&FS and the World
Bank's policies (O.D. 4.30 and 4.20).
With regard to Social Assessment, the above emphasizes that the
population displaced and/or adversely affected receive benefits from
the project and that where displacement is unavoidable, the project
should assist with the means to improve the former living standards,
income generating capacities and production levels or at least
E                         maintain the previous standards of living of those displaced and
adversely affected. This requires a thorough understanding of the
social, economic and cultural conditions of the area and its people
and the factors which influence the lives of those affected. It is in this
context that a detailed socio-economic study of the project area and
its people has been undertaken by the Consultants.
7.3         Study Process
The process of social assessment involves primarily the following
steps:
1.    General Assessment: This process involves enlisting
chainagewise all the PAPs, Public structures, Trees (fruit
bearing and non-fruit bearing) belonging to the land owners
etc. and their exact distances from the central line of the
existing ROW. The outcome of this exercise gives the idea
about the chainagewise amount of the losses involved in the
project implementation and serves as the first order
approximation to the final rsk calculating exercise.
2.    Identification and Profiling of PAPs   This process is
followed by general assessment wherein Head of every
Project Affected Family (PAF) is interviewed with respect to his
socio-economic aspects thoroughly and the responses are
recorded in a detailed questionnaire especially designed for
this survey. This exercise generates a very important and
useful database for the further analysis and decision making.
3.    Development of Draft RAP: After analyzing the information
collected in the identification and profiling of PAP exercise,
draft version of Resettlement Action Plan is developed.
Kirloakar Consultants Limited
7-2



En vironmental and Social Assessment Report                      Chapte
Vadodara - Halol Road                          Social Assessment - An Introduction
4.    Public Information and Consultations: Keeping in view the
draft version of RAP, an exercise is initiated (1) to understand
in depth the sentiments and the feelings of the people losing
their assets in the project and (2) to identify the social and
environmental issues/problems at large so that no such issue
remains unaddressed as well as to take care of the problems
as perceived by the people losing their assets due to project
implementations.
5.    Finalization of RAP: The RAP is finalized based on the draft
version of the RAP and findings of ihe PIC exercise.
6.    Identification of Institutional Arrangement : As the final
step  of  SA,  institutional  arrangements  for  efficient
implementation of RAP and monitoring of its outcome are
suggested.
7.4          Progression of the Study
The social assessment study was initiated in August 1996 and the
focus was on the general assessment, identification and profiling of
the structure PAPs. This phase of study revealed that major stretch of
the project road is linear and very few structures fall in the proposed
ROW except for the villages Jarod, Asoj in Vadodara district and
village Baska in Panchamahals district. At this stage it was suggested
by the Consultants that:
- except for the villages Jarod, Asoj and Baska, minimize the ROW,
wherever possible, in order to minimize the losses in structures
and to keep ROW sufficiently away from most of the structures;
and
- at villages Jarod , Asoj and Baska the bypasses be introduced to
ensure minimized losses of structures, to ensure increased safety
and to maintain better speed geometrics.
The suggestions were well accepted and finalized at IL&FS, New
Delhi in October 1996 in consultation with Roads and Buildings
Department (R&B) and Government of Gujarat (GoG). While technical
and environmental consultantsWere working on the design of new
bypasses, social assessment study focused on identification and
-profiling of land PAPs and the task was accomplished during
November 1996. During the same time, technical and environmental
consultants designed the bypasses. The demarcation of the central
line for the proposed three bypasses was initiated by technical
consultants along with Public Works Department (PWD) officials in
January 1997 and the task was completed by February 1997.
Followed by the demarcation exercise, social assessment was started
at these three villages with respect to identification and profiling of
structures and land owner PAPs and also to assess the social risks
involved in acquiring the land for the new alignments.
A set of PIC was conducted in March 1997 to assess the project
awareness among the villagers of the project area, to inform them
about the project design, to understand their general views, opinions,
Kirloskar Consultants Umited
7-3



- ., .. , f g . .~Environmenwi ard SoilAssessment R,port                          w.Raper-
- -;  - 0 00:            Vadodara -,iol Road                             Social Assement- An introduction
sentiments pertaining to the project, to identify major issues and
issues in commonality and obtain consent on draft R&R measures. As
an outcome of PIC exercise, the alignment of Asoj bypass was
modified to minimize the impact due to land acquisition to the land
owners. Also a number of road crossings were added to meet the
community needs at appropriate locations.
7.5          Benefits From The Project
Although a small portion of population in the project area is affected
directly due to the property and land acquisition, a larger population in
the project area and in surrounding regions is expected to gain
significantly from the project.
As the main objective of the project is to improve the service level for
traffic, all the road users, as well as the general population and the
PAPs, will be benefited from the proposed improvement on account of
comfort, safety, reduced travel time and better economic
opportunities. The society will be benefited economically from the
saving on vehicle operating costs due to enhanced speed, better
geometrics, smooth surface, etc. The project will upgrade linkages to
national highways and open up the area for increased economic
activities.
Also during the construction phase, a significant economic benefit of
the project is the generation of employment opportunities which will be
available to the people, including the affected community. Besides,
they will also draw benefits from the economic activities as a result of
increased traffic flow and movement of vehicles. Local communities
will have greater access to the public and other transport facilities.
The most significant impact of the project is the reduction in the
number of accidents and fatalities due to enhanced safety conditions.
The subsequent chapters present an overview of the prevailing socio-
economic conditions in the project area, detail the legal frame work
and land and property acquisition, discuss the social assessment
process and PIC process. Final chapter details the RAP emerging
from the above process, including the institutional arrangement for its
implementation.
Kirlaskar Consultants Limited

8.1         Socio-Economic Background Of The State And The Project Area
Districts
The socio-economic parameters are the major determinants of the
growth in the transport demand. These aspects have been reviewed
in the pre-feasibility study carried out for the project and identified
Vadodara - Panchmahal, the project districts, -as the regions with
potential growth in transport demand due to the rapid industrialization
of the region. In this section an overview of the socio-economic profile
of the state and project districts is presented as a background to the
Resettlement Action Plan.
Gujarat with a population of 41.3 million, ranks tenth in population and
ninth in area amongst the states of India as per 1991 census. Of the
total population 34.49% belong to urban areas and the state ranks
fourth in urban population among the states in the country. The
female to male (F/M) ratio in 1991 was 936:1000 for the state
914:1000 for Vadodara and 953:1000 for Panchmahals. The literacy
level of the state and the project districts have been increasing over a
period of time. From a meager 35% literacy rate in 1971 it has
increased to 51% in the state. The projects districts have also shown
remarkable increase in the literacy levels. The female literacy rate has
almost doubled during 1971-1991 in the state and in the project
districts.
Nearly 40.23% of the state's population was engaged in some activity
or the other and hence were called workers. The work participation of
the total population among males at 53.57% was higher than females
at 25.96%. The dependency rate is higher in urban areas (with
30.22% of urban population being workers) than in rural areas (work
participation rate being 45.51%). Of the total workers, 34.12% were
main workers (with an engagement for more than 183 days) and the
rest 6.11%   constituted  marginal workers. The  demographic
characteristics of the state and the project districts are given in Table -
8.1.
Table - 8.1

Agriculture which is a major economic activity has declined during the
last 30 years and the state has gradually been transformed from an
agriculture based economy to a major industrial force. Decline in the
proportion of workforce dependent on agriculture, stagnation in the
area under cultivation, marg.nal increase in the area under irrigation
have been the characteristic features of Gujarat. Of the total
workforce about 70% was dependent on agriculture in 1971 and this
decreased to 60% in 1991b-Area under cultivation -remained stagnant
at 47 to 48% during 1980-81 and 1990-91. Vadodara, the district, in
which large section of the proposed road passes through, has
becoming a major industrial area. In Vadodara, 57% of the workforce
engaged in primary (agriculture) sector. However, area under
cultivation and irrigation is relatively high indicating relative agriculture
richness of the region. However, district Panchmahals continue to be
agro based with more than 80% of workforce dependent on primary
activity. The district being. largely populated with tribals, low irrigation
levels, the agriculture in the district is at subsistence level. Table - 8.2
presents the agricultural indicators of the project districts.

Gujarat has become the second largest industrial state in the country
in 1990, from the initial eighth position in 1960. Gujarat accounted for
10.64% share of value of production and 10.08% share in value
addition, in the factory sector of the country. The manufacturing sector
contributes 34% of net domestic product of Gujarat in 1991. The
factory sector registered an annual compound growth rate of 4.7% in
the past three decades with employment growing around 2.6%. Table-
8.3 gives number of working factories in Gujarat and in project
districts.

-The consistent efforts over the past three decades made Gujarat the
second largest industrial state. The state has been attracting large
investments. As of now, about 2,200 projects with an estimated
investment of Rs. 899.73 billion generating 420,000 jobs are under
implementation in Gujarat. Among the project districts Vadodara tops
with a share of 17% of projects, whereas Panchmahals share is 3.5%.
The socio-economic parameters of the state and the project districts
indicate that the state has achieved remarkable growth in industrial
sector. The growth in industrial sector will further increase the demand
for transport and communications sectors in future.

8.2          Socio-Economic Aspects Of The Villages In The Project Area
As stated earlier, VHRP covers two districts - Vadodara and
Panchamahal. Large section of Vadodara Halol road passes through
the district of Vadodara. Of the proposed 31.5 Kms road, 25.9 Kms
passes through Vadodara district and the remaining 5.6 Kms comes
in Panchamahal district.
8.2.1        Methodology Adopted For The Study
The methodology adopted for the study is based on the following:
*     Villages on the either side of the existing alignment have been
considered for socio-economic studies. According to 1991
census, there are 14 villages in the study area. All these villages
- -           form a part of the rural area despite being very near to the urban
area.
*     Review of secondary data was done for the above 14 villages
from 1991 and 1981 District Census, Gazetteers, Distnct
Statistical Hand-books, Urban development plans with respect
to population, occupational structure and infrastructure facilities
available in the region.
8.2.2        Review Of Socio-Economic Profile - 1991
The information on socio-economic aspects of the study area has been
compiled from secondary sources. The demographic data has mainly
been compiled from Census Handbook, 1991. The sociological aspects
of this study include human settlements, demographic and other socio-
economic aspects and infrastructural facilities available in the study
area. The economic aspects include agriculture, industry and
occupational structure of workers. The socio-economic details are
briefly described in the following sections.
Demography
a)    Settlement Pattem
As per the village bounda"rmaps depicted in 1991 Census handbooks,
there are 12 villages in Vadodara and 2 in Panchmahal districts,
therefore 14 villages in total falling within the study area. The
geographical distribution of all the settlements are given in Table - 8.4.

8.3          Minimisation Of Adverse Social Impact
As a prelude to the socio-economic survey, consultants carried out a
verification' exercise of the entire corridor of impact of the project
mainly to identify any structures coming within the proposed road
widening. Most of the area within the corridor of impact was' found
under private use for housing, business and other economic activities.
The verification exercise found the following, types of assets coming
within the corridor of impact:
1. Temporary structures -(thatched roof, wooden cabins etc.):
2. Semi-permanent structures (tiled/asbestos roof houses with mud
walls),
3. Permanent structures (tiled/asbestos roof with brick I stone walls,
RCC / RBC structures etc.);
4. Other structures like boundary walls (brick / stones), fencing, etc.;
and
5. Agricultural land.
The initial alternatives were generated solely on the basis of
engineering design requirements to meet the level of service
requirements. These alternatives included strengthening the existing
carriageway going through the villages by reducing the ROW to 36 m
without compromising on the design speed. This also gave the choice
of acquiring the additional land on either left or right side 'of the
existing road.
The verification exercise identified 594 structures in 31.5 Kms road
length. These consisted of 225 structures being used for residential
*                                 purposes, 245 commercial establishments, 59 community / public
infrastructure (including 19 temples) and 65 other structures. This
would have resulted in a major relocation problem in addition to large
amounts of compensation payable to the owners of the structures. At
the same time this would have significantly disturbed the economic
living of the people. In fact, a detailed socio-economic survey was
carried out by consultants-.-mong the people living within the corridor
of impact. Group discussions were also held by its professionals with
local population in the villages mainly to gauge their feelings on the
proposed road improvement.
The consultants sensed that the resistance to the project might build
up creating problems during the construction phases of the project.
Therefore, attempts were made to minimize the problems of the
displacement by modifying the road alignment. Since 395 structures
(66.5 %) of the total structures (594) are coming in only three villages
(Jarod. Asoj and Baska), it was proposed to bypass in these villages
and accordingly the project road was realigned. The proposed lengths
of these three bypasses are 1.458 kms., 1.52 kms. and 1.16 kms.
respectively (total bypasses' length is 4.138 kms. against 4.08 kms.
for alignment passing through three villages).
Kirloskar consultants Limitd
6-10



Enwonmentfl. and Soial Awssssment Repo                      B      an
Vadodara-Hafol Road                              Soeio-conomic Backgroundand
Focus area for Social Impact Assessment
At another enroute village Khandewadi, the land acquisition for the
proposed ROW of 60 m width entailed demolition of.a settlement of
huts (44) adjacent to the road on the west and redamation of about
10% of the area from a pond on the east of the highway. Avoidance of
the settlement by shifting the additional land acquisition for the
proposed ROW to the east would have increased the reclamation of
the pond area to 22%. Considering the associated risks of flooding
during the monsoon, this was not considered to be desirable. The
resettlement issues at the stretch were minimized by reducing the
proposed ROW of 60 rn to 36 m, the minimum width required to
maintain the road design parameters, and acquiring the land on the
east of the existing carriageway.
The IL&FS officials in the spirit of IL&FS ESA policy and World Bank
O.D. 4.20 reviewed the results and desired to minimize the
resettlement problem. The Consultants, in consultation with IL&FS
officials and GoG officials, adopted the above measures which led to
minimization of the resettlement as under:
1. The resettlement owing to demolition of residential structures has
-*    .                                been reduced from 225 PAFs to 10 PAFs;
2. The requirement of restoration of commercial structures has been
minimized from 245 to mobile 11 units, which can be pushed
back;
3. The requirement of shifting of temples has been reduced from 19
to 7; and
_Z7                                4. The overall dislocation of other public and community structures
also has been minimized.
8.4         Right Of Way And Road Improvement
Right of Way (ROW) refers to the public land and is under the control
of Roads and Buildings Department (R&B). The existing ROW is 24
m. The proposed widenin% and road improvements require 60 m wide
strip of land at the straight sections and upto 70 m wide strip at the
curves. This additional area between the existing ROW and land
-                         required for the widened and improved road is defined as "Corridor of
Impact" and forms the basis for identification of PAPs.
8.5         Land Acquisition For Widening Of ROW
Since the exisfing ROW is 24 m and the proposed road width after
widening is 60 m, a strip of 36 m needs to be acquired all along
sections which run along the existing road. The proposed road will
however, deviate from the -existing alignment at Jarod, Asoj and
Baska villages. The bypasses to these villages will have a combined
length of 4.138 kms. The R&B and Land Acquisition (LA) department
have jointly identified the land to be acquired in both the districts
through Joint Measurement Survey (JMS). The District-wise break-up
of land required for the project is provided in Table - 8.9.

8.6          Impact On Vulnerable Sections
According  to  ESR  of IL&FS, vulnerable  sections are  tribals
(indigenous people), women, aged, orphans and disabled. Though
Panchmahals is predominantly characterized by presence of tribals,
the project road is of 5.6 kms in this district. It passes through an
industrial belt.and there are no tribal habitations in the study area.
Since the project may not result in any displacement of population,
impact on vulnerable sections and communities will be confined to
economical impacts. Further, the project being linear in nature and
spread through out the narrow corridor of impact, oniy a narrow strip
of land will be required from an individual land owner on an average.
This will not result in major landlessness in the area and thus
economic earning of those dependent on such lands including
agriculture labour also will not be affected significantly. There is
however still a need to identify vulnerable members for each PAF and
provide for mitigation measures in RAP.

9
Legal Framework for Land and
Property Acquisition



Entvronmntal ard Soci Assessment Rewot                             Chapter4
Vadodara-HaloI Road                   Legal Framework for Land and Property Acquisition
9.1          Land Acquisition
All land acquisition (LA) for any public purpose in the state is
governed by the Land Acquisition Act 1894 amended in 1984. It is a
Central Act and empowers the government to acquire compulsorily
any land not owned by it which is required for a public utility. Land
acquisition applies to those with legal entitlements to land and
structures thereon. In this RAP these are referred to as 'title holders'
of land and other properties there on.
In accordance with this Act:
.     Compensation for land can be awarded by granting 'land for
land'. This is granted by the Collector in particular
circumstances.
Where compensation  of privately  owned  lands, trees,
structures and other assets is paid in money, it is provided at
the  prevailing  'market value'. According  to  the  Land
Acquisition Manual, market value is the price which a willing
*                              vendor might reasonably expect to obtain from  a willing
purchaser. In addition to market value a sum equivalent to
30% of the value of compensation, known as solatium, is paid
in consideration of the compulsory nature of the acquisition.
Interest is paid on the compensation and solatium amount
from the date of notification of the land acquisition or the date
of taking possession of the land, whichever is the earliest.
If there are any disputes relating to the fixation of rates these
will be referred to the court for settlement.
If the land acquisition results in a holding to be operationally
non-viable, the  entire  holding  wilf be  acquired  and
compensated accordingly. This could include land exchanged
for land.
9.2          Procedure For Land Acquisition
For any LA, the Act under section (u/s) - 4(1) stipulates publication of
a notification to that effect in the official Gazette and in two daily
newspapers circulated in the locality of which at least one shall be in
the regional language. Such a notification enables the concerned
authorities to enter upon the land, make preliminary survey, ascertain
its suitability and determine the exact portion of the land to be
acquired. The substance of this notification is given as public notice at
a convenient place in the locality in which the land to be acquired is
located. Such a notification provides an opportunity to the parties
interested in the land to file objections if any, u1s-5A to the proposed
acquisition.
In order to proceed with the acquisition of the notified land or any
portion of it, a declaration u/s-6 of the Act specifies the precise
boundaries of the area of the land to be taken. Every declaration is
published in the official Gazette and in two daily newspapers with
Klrloskar consultants Limited
9X1



enmwinmenrai Wan oocuw ^Assasmenr  n                             .ap 
Vadodara-Habto Road                  Legal Framowork for Land and Propty Acqulsaban
circulation in the local area. With the appearance of the notification
u/s-6 of the Act in the Gazette, Government in Revenue Department.-
issues direction to the Collector u/s - 7 of the Act to take order for the
acquisition of notified land and/or other immovable properties.
Thereafter, Section-8 of the Act requires land to be marked out,
measured and planned, while u/s-9 notices are served -to the
concemed persons stating the intention of the Govemment to take
possession of the land and that any claims for compensation should
be made to the Collector. Finally, the award of compensation is made
by the Collector u/s-11 of the Act after inquiring into objections (if any)
in pursuant to the notice given u/s-9, to the measurements made u s-
8 and into the value of the land on the date of publication of the
notification u/s 4(1). The award u/s - 11 is made within two years from
the date of publication of the declaration and if no award is made
within this period, the entire proceedings of the LA is lapsed and fresh
notification u/s 4(1) needs to be initiated. Once the award is passed,
the Collector takes possession of the land, which there upon vests
absolutely in the Govemment free from all encumbrances. However.
in case of urgency Section-17 of the Act empowers the Collector to
take possession of the land even though no award has been mace.
Such land thereupon vests with the Govemment free from all
encumbrances.
9.3          R&R Policy
R&R policy for the project has been formulated in accordance with the
World Bank Guidelines on Involuntary Resettlement (OD 4.30) and
Environment and Social Assessment policy of the IL&FS. The VHRP
policy takes into consideration those coming within the 'corridor of
negative impact' of the project. Essentially they are in two broad
categories.
*     those with private ownership of land and other immovable
assets; and
encroachers on govemment land and squatters within the
corridor of impact.
Definition Of A PAP      All persons affected by the VHRP
implementation through acquisition of land and other properties or
removal of squatters and eviction of encroachers from the 'corridor
of impact' or indirectly by the loss of any economic opportunities due
to the project is considered as a Project Affected Person (PAP). And
a PAP displaced as a result of acquisition or removal of structures
from the 'corridor of impact' is considered as a displaced person or
displaced business depending upon the purpose for which the
structure is being used.
Cut Off Date      The cut-off date for the PAPs losing their
privately owned land and structures outside the public land (ROW) for
the project will be date of the notification u/s 4(1) of LA Act. In case of
persons losing access to land and other structures within the ROW,
the cut off date for their eligibility for R&R assistance is the date of the
Kirloksar Consultants Limimd
9-2



Environmental and SociaAssessment                    :                         hapter-9p
Vadodara-HaIlo Road                          Legal Framework for Land and Property Acquisition
socio - economic survey in the area and for VHRP this cut off date is
October 1996.
Entitlements          The main objective of deriving the entitlement
through compensation and assistance to be given to the PAPs during
R&R is to ensure that their standards of living is at least restored to
the pre-project level and preferably improved above the levels they
enjoyed before the implementation of the project. In the absence of
any legal regulatory framework to govern such entitlements
associated with resettlement and rehabilitation projects, IL&FS has
adopted its own standards for entitlement framework as furnished
below in Table - 9.1.

10.1         Coverage Of Socio-Economic Profile Survey Of PAPs
The objective of conducting a socio-economic survey of the PAPs was
to ascertain their pre-project socio-economic status:
*  to ensure that the entitlement for each PAP is in line with the R&R
policy; and
*  to provide the baseline economical status for post-project
monitoring of the implementation of the R&R measures.
The decision to minimize the R&R issues through project design
modification, as discussed earlier in section 8.3, significantly reduced
the extent of coverage required in the socio-economic survey for
PAPs loosing commercial structures due to acquisition of land for the
project. The generation of such profiles was undertaken by
categorizing the PAPs as under:
(1) Land holders;
(2) Tenants working on agricultural lands; and
(3) PAPs loosing residential/commercial structures but not falling
under categories (1) or (2) above.
Since the maximum impact of LA is on PAPs loosing land to the
project (492 plots), it was felt necessary to focus on their socio-
economic profile in detail through household surveys of the PAFs.
However, due to the difficulties experienced in identifying the land
owners, as the land records were not updated, the survey was
undertaken for those 50 PAFs (owning 66 plots) who could be
correctly identified as the land owners. This needs to be followed up
by survey of each PAF subsequently when the correct status of the
land ownership is established.
In the case of tenants working on agricultural lands, these people
manage the lands on a sea:-onal (or longer) basis. The period of
observation was in non-cropping season and therefore the tenants
were not available at the agricultural fields. As their place of residence
and location of the agricultural-Wands is often different and they were
engaged in other occupation during the non-cropping period, the
tenants could not be contacted. At this stage, by extensive inquiries
with villagers, a partial list of land tenants which could be compiled is
given in Annexure IV.
In case of 10 PAPs loosing residential structures, their socio-economic
profiling has been done as presented in Annexure V-A.
A complete profiling of all the remaining commercial and public
3104                            structure damage/loss related 71 PAPs has been presented in
Annexure V-B. They fall in the following categories:
* movable shops (11 nos.);
*  damage to fencing and compound wall;

*  community structures like bus stop, temples, water wells and
toilets.
The details of the socio-economic profile are given in the subsequent
sections. These details then form the basis for the entitlement
package for each PAP in keeping with the IL&FS R&R policy which is

female/male ratio works out to be 925/1000. As can be seen from the
following information, population in the age group of 4-18 years,
representing those going to school for education, constitutes 22.6%
while that in the age-group 18-60 years, which forms the workforce,
accounts for 59%. In India and it is also true for the project area, the
people in the periphery of workforce i.e. those in the age-groups of 5-
18 years and 60+ group particularly among weaker sections also offer
their labour to supplement household income.

Literacy
A little less than two-thirds of the PAPs, are literate which is higher
than the state's average (51%) reported during the 1991 census. One
of the reasons for the present higher literacv rate in the area is the
concerted govemment efforts to promote education among the
illiterate masses under the programmes of National Literacy Mission
which includes non-formal adult literacy, universalization of primary
education, increase in the number of resideitial schools, supply of
free text books etc. However, the disparir; in the literacy rates

Workinc PoDulation
It has already been observed that around 59- of the population falls
under the age-group 18-60 years which generally is considered as the
working age group. As against this, we find that only 37.3% population
is engaged in some economic activity. Considering the semi-urban
nature of the life in the project area and limited farm based activities,
employment opportunities to the females are reported to be less. As a
result, while 60.3% of males are found pursuing some activity or the
other for economic gains, the same is as low 9.3% among females.
Since agriculture in the area is predominantly rainfed, the returns from
the farm do not ensure the sustenance of the family specially for
those land owners who have holdings below or about minimum
economic land holdings (MEL). As a result, these families also seek
other avenues to supplement their household income. Even the

Agriculture In The Area
The LA for VHRP will affect land holders. Hardly 8% of the land
owned by people in the area is irrigated mainly through wells. As a
result only one crop is taken up. The major crops in the area are
Jowar, Cotton and Paddy in-the monsoon. Pulses are grown where
soils have better moisture retention capacity. Cultivation is practiced in
the area with improved agronomic practices. In fact cotton, a
commercial crop in the area helps in increased farm eamings.

Sources of Income
During the socio-economic study it was found that land PAPs have
reported their agricultural income on the lower side. Any analysis
based on the agricuitural income will not allow any meaningful
conclusions. The non-agricultural income appeared to have been
reported more correctly, hence was used in the analysis to determine
the extent and dependability of other sources of income. The analysis
of non-agricultural income revealed that  out of 47 PAFs who
responded to the income related queries
* 45% of the PAFs reported to have no other source of income
therefore indicating major dependence on agricultural income;
- 38% of the-PAFs have income which falls above the poverty line
(inspite of questionable response to agricultural income status)
indicating sufficient livelihood avenues; and
* remaining 17% of the families appear to have income below the
line of poverty, but in reality, need to be verified as the agricultural
income may not be correctly reported.

10.2.3      Principles For Identifying Land Loss Related Entitlement
The following principles will be followed while arriving at the
entitlements for three categories of PAPs mentioned below:
Cateaory 1: PAPs loosing land which results in their balance land
after LA being above MEL:
Cash compensation will be provided at market value which allows the
PAP to purchase laad elsewhere or invest the compensation in fixed
deposits to earn a relular income.
Categorv 2:  PAPs having land above MEL before LA but below MEL
after LA:
In such cases, additional land adjacent to the current land will be
procured, if available, so as to leave him with a MEL after the project.
The balance land (difference between his original land holding and
MEL provided) will be compensated by cash. In case procurement of
additional adjacent lands to bring the post LA land holding of the
PAP up to MEL is not possible, the PAP will be provided alternate
contiguous land equal to the MEL elsewhere. Under such
circumstances, the entire land holding of the PAP will be acquired by
the project. The PAP will also receive cash compensation for the
balance lands as described above.
Cateqorv 3: PAPs having land below MEL and part of it needs to be
acquired:
All his land will be procured and he will be provided contiguous land,
equal to the MEL, elsewhere, i.e. land for land option will be the first
priority. In addition rehabilitation measures will be identified and
implemented to ensure that the post LA income level for such PAFs
remain above a threshold income level necessary for sustainance.
The minimum income level of Rs. 22,400 equivalent to two times the
poverty line (currently defined Rs. 11200 per year at 1991 prices) is
considered as the threshold level for VHRP.
General Considerations for Raising Post-LA Income to Pre-LA Income
Level:
- In case of all three categories above, if the post-LA income level
(including the accrued interest eamings from investment of the cash
compensation as well as other sources of income) tails below the pre-
LA income level, the options to increase the post-LA income to pre-
LA  levels are considered as obligatory while developing the
entitlement package for the PAF. The additional income generation
schemes will be identified and implemented through financial aid
and/or loan. The financial assistance necessary to raise the post-LA
income level to the threshold level, if applicable, (if it is below the
threshold level) will be offered as a grant and the remaining amount
necessary to further increase the income to the pre-LA levels will be
provided as a loan. The vocational training needs to make optimum
Kirloskar Consultants Umited
ltL7



En-:ronmental and Social Assewnent Report                      Chapter-10
Vadodara-Halol Road                  Soco-economIc Profile of the PAPs and Draft RAP
use of grant/loan to start a business will be identified and provided for
the project with the help of a NGO in consultation with the PAP.
Other Guidina Considerations:
Outlined below are the general guidelines which will be followed while
identifying the individual PAP entitlement.
* Land for land option will be the first priority in the case of those
PAPs whose only livelihood means is cultivation and their land
holding after LA is below MEL.
*  The extent of compensatory land to be provided (as detailed in
categories - 1 through 3) will be applicable to the family as a larger
unit. The land title rights will be non-alienable and in the case of
married persons be held jointly by the husband and wife. However,
while deciding the entitlement for assistance towards income
generating schemes, each major son will be considered as a
separate family unit.
- Management of LA Compensation Funds: The compensation
amount will be deposited in long term deposits in the name of the
PAP and they will be paid accrued interest which will add up to the
other sources of income so that it is equivalent to their pre-project
income. In no case will the compensation be paid directly and in
cash.. The management of this fund shall be supervised by the
NGO. Persons who produce a viable plan of building productive
assets, subject to the approval of the NGO, may be released the
money as per requirement.
10.3        Tenants Working on Agricultural Lands
Land tenants are given land by the land owners on lease for a
season/year or even for a longer period to utilise this land for
cultivation. This forms their means of livelihood. With land acquisition
proposed under the project, not only the land owners but also land
tenants are affected.
As discussed in section 10.1, the land tenants are only partially
identified. The changing nature of association of the land tenants with
the land owners needs to be recognized because:
*  natural justice should be given to those land tenants who have
been cultivating the lands for a long period and are left with no
other source of income due to LA;
* there can be a genuine change of a land tenant, due to which a
land tenant identified at one point in time (say a cut-off date
corresponding to the notification under section 4) for R&R and the
land tenant at the time of physical LA (not known/identified earlier)
are different, then a fair criteria needs to be evolved to decide
eligibility for R&R; and
Kirloskar Consultants Linited
10-8



En- ronrmuetl and Sodal Assessment Repot                       Chaptmr.1O
__           Vadodara-HaAol Road                  Soco-economic Profile of the PAPs and Draft RAP
* land owners may also pose a land tenant for additional benefits
which is not in keeping with the spirit of R&R.
Hence the exercise of correctly and completely identifying eligible land
tenants needs to be undertaken through NGO by an in-depth
investigation for deciding the eligibility and entitlement. Certain norms
for deciding eligibility and guidelines for formulating the entitlement
package for land tenants is presented below:
A) Land tenants' information needs to be collected by the NGO by
closely interacting with the villagers, requesting tenants to contact
the NGO through public meetings/notices, paying visits to the
actual land (to be acquired) site and meeting land owners.
Information about the date of the land tenant lease must be
collected and cross-checked.
B) The cut-off date be treated as the date of notification of LA under
section 4(1) i.e. October 1996. Only those land tenants, who
maintain this status since the cut-off date till the date of LA, will be
considered eligible for entitlement. All other cases - such as a
person being a land tenant on the cut-off date but who has found
a substitute occupation, or a new land tenant after the cut-off date
- will not be considered eligible for entitlement.
C) Conduct socio-economic survey of the eligible tenants identffied
and based on their income levels and land available for cultivation
as tenants, they should be categorised in to the following.
a) Marginally affected: Those tenants who are left with more
than MEL to cultivate or have other sources of income so that
they are above the threshold income level can be put in to this
category. As post-LA income is likely to be below pre-LA level,
financial assistance necessary to increase the income to the pre-
LA levels will be provided as a loan. The vocational training needs
to make optimum use of loan to start a business will be identified
and provided for by the project with the help of a NGO in
consultation with the PAP.
b) Moderately affected: Those tenants who have less than MEL
left for cultivation and cannot sustain themselves due to loss in
income from cultivation but have non-agricultural skills and
eamings, can be put in to this category. Financial assistance, as
a grant, to improve the income level above the threshold level and
rest as loan to restore the present income levels will be provided.
The vocational training needs to make optimum use of grant/loan
to start a business will be identified and provided for by the project
with the help of a NGO in consultation with the PAP.
c) Severely affected: Those tenants who are fully dependent on
cultivation and have less than MEL left for cultivation, due to
which they will fall below the threshold level due to LA, can be put
into this category. Preferably MEL equivalent cultivable land area
be provided. Else, financial assistance as a grant to improve
income level above threshold level will be provided.

government land for constructing the house. The PAPs will be

which they will be paid wages. Further, if desired, the PAPs

* The movable structures comprise tea stalls/pan shops on

Vadodara-Haloi Road                            Public information and Consultation
Finalisation of R&R Action Plan
11.0         Public Information And Consultation
:                     11.1        Introduction
The quantitative aspect of social assessment process is not enough
to ensure a responsive R.A.P. This is mainly because of the fact that
this exercise addresses an individual at a time during the study and
not a group of individuals or a community. Therefore, to arrive at
meaningful conclusions, a qualitative social assessment has to be
exercised wherein group of representative individuals come together
and express their opinions about the project. Keeping this view in
mind, Public Information and Consultations (PIC) exercise was
conducted with following aim:
* to understand in depth the sentiments and feelings of the people
losing their assets in the project;
* to identify the social and environmental issues/problem at large so
that no such issue remains unaddressed; and
*  to resolve satisfactorily problems as perceived by the people
losing their assets due to project implementation.
As a fruifful outcome of this exercise, misconceptions about the
project implementation where removed, number of underpasses,
possible solutions (including constraints) addressing the social
concems were discussed/evaluated consensus was developed about
the alignment especially the by passes so that land structure loss
would not reduce the utility of the balance unacquired land (as in case
of Asoj bypass) and a number of road crossings (underpasses or at
grade crossings) were introduced to limit the severance especially in
the case of cart tracks or pathways.
11.2        PIC Process
Since the proposed project is linear in nature and very few structures
come in the proposed ROW except for the villages Jarod, Asoj and
Baska, it was decided to conduct PIC in
*  two villages which are densely populated viz. Bhaniyara and Halol;
*  three villages Jarod, AsoT& Baska, where bypasses are proposed;
and
* Khandewadi village located adjacent to the existing highway and a
unique feature of loss of pond area was to occur due to the
proposed alignment.
The major issues were anticipated in these six villages for obvious
reasons. In each of these villages a group of farmers, land owners,
businessmen, agricultural labourers, village Tahsildar, Gram
Panchayat officials, women and teachers were selected to cover
socio-economic cross-section of the village. Enough care was taken
also to include some of the PAPs of the project in each group. The
group was also selected well in advance and the time schedule of PIC
was informed to them three days prior to conducting PIC in the
respective village. A pamphlet in Gujarati language (local language)
Kirloskar Consultants Umited
t11_t



Enrronrnentgl and Social AssessmJet Report                     .nyaprar-77
Vadodara-Halo Road                             Public 1ntomatIon and Consultation
Finalisation of R&R Action Plan
was carefully prepared detailing major aspects and concems of this -
project from social and environmental view. The pamphlets were
distributed to the group members ten minutes before the PIC.
Each PIC was initiated by detailing of the project needs and its
justification by giving village specific observations made throughout
this study to the group. These observations were mainly pertaining to
traffic projections, reduced safety and environmental aspects in the
absence and presence of the project, environmental resources such
as pond etc. This was followed by the description about the social &
environmental  mitigation  process  planned  in  the  project
implementation. Also the larger economic prospects were highlighted
in these intersections. This established an important linkage between
the group and the consultants through an informal and friendly
atmosphere creation which allowed the group members to freely
discuss their concems.
All the members of the group were invited to express themselves in
various capacities. The opinions of the group members were
extremely useful for the development of a consensus for the
alignment as a whole after classifying and analyzing each and every
group member's opinion. Various alternatives for mitigation measures
on general environmental & social issues were also worked out.
Also on an individual basis wherever possible alignment of road was
given a thought for all possible modifications.
A number of PAP-specific mitigation measures were evolved,
discussed, evaluated and documented on the spot. Major social and
land acquisition related issues were discussed at an individual basis
and perceived need based solutions were suggested.
Thus the PIC process resulted in a general understanding of the
project needs and constraints and gave confidence to the group that
they were important partners in the development process. A win-win
situation wherever feasible was evolved which is intended to serve as
a guideline while developing the RAP.

The village is fourth largest village enroute the project road and has a

north of the road. The majority of agricultural lands belonging to the
village, however, lie south of the project road requiring frequent road
crossings by the villagers. The villagers were most concerned about
the land and property acquisition. Second to property issues were the
concerns about the severance of village from the agricultural lands.

adequate compensation for land and property

'-'9                                    continued use of the present modes/places of road crossing:

the end (100 m before the petrol pump).
c.    The possibility of providing employment in addition to the
compensation given for land acquisition was requested.
d.    Clarifications- were requested on the policy of toll collection
from the users residing in the on-route villages or those who
have land in these villages but stay in Vadodara.
e.    A follow-up meeting was requested to discuss the above
issues
During the PIC, the villagers were told that a humane and just
entitlement package, including rehabilitation measures where
necessary, will be worked out for each project affected person (PAP)
through a participatory process with adequate representation of PAPs
and community representatives. Villagers were told that the project as
such did not have much--scope for direct employment. The
rehabilitation measures, however, will be developed through
professional advise and will be compatible with the skills and market
conditions in the project area. One road crossing at the village is
considered essential and the consultants have been asked to propose
a cost effective solution

11.3.2      Jarod (Location: Kms on SH 87 from Golden Chowkey on NH-8)
Venue: M.P. High School
Number of persons present for the meeting :22
The village is largest village enroute the project road and has a
population of. The villagers were aware about the project and
proposed bypass. The main concern was the possible loss of
business due to construction of the project road bypassing the village
resulting in diversion of traffic away from the village. At the beginning
of the meeting while expressing their concerns, villagers expressed
preference for maintaining the present alignment and not to bypass
the village. Villagers also expressed strong opposition to the bypass
due to the loss of agricultural property and a housing scheme in the    i
advanced stage of planning
Some village representatives, -however, brought out the fact that
village economy had considerable dependence on transport business
arising due to locally owned transport vehicles which required parking
and repair/maintenance facilities. They expressed a concem that if the
road passed through the village, the transport business will suffer as
there will not be any space left along the road for parking the vehicles
The village community, thus, was clearly divided into two groups -
general business persons and people losing land due to bypass
favoured retention of present alignment; whereas persons related to
transport business favoured the bypass. Alternate alignments for
bypass to minimise loss of land and property were also suggested
During the PIC, the first objective, therefore, was to evolve a
consensus on road alignment. The findings on negative environmental
and social impacts of retaining the existing alignments for the widened
road in terms of increased noise and air pollution, difficulties in road
crossing and loss of a large number of structures were explained. It
was also explained that the traffic volumes on the project road were
likely to increase many folds in future years and the number of
vehicles halting (passenger traffic, local truckers and locally generated
traffic) at the village were not expected to reduce from the present    4
lever. It was also told to the community representatives that adequate
entitlement package addressing losses and needs of individual PAPs
will developed through a participatory. process. The consultations
helped in reducing the concern for loss of business and property and
helped in reaching a consensus in favour of the bypass.
After achieving the consensus on bypass requirement and alignment
(technical requirements for achieving the design speeds were
explained) the issues related to land and property acquisition along
the proposed bypass were discussed. The concems of all present
landowners were individually discussed and mutually acceptable
course of action arrved at. The outcomes with reference to the
location specific plots are presented below
